KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Gutierrez v. Holder, 9th Cir., January 20, 2011
2008 WL 3861940 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARTIN CALDERON GUTIERREZ A.K.A. MARTIN CALTERON
File: AXX XX4 876 - San Francisco, CA
July 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Micheal K. Mehr, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ozlem B. Barnard, Esquire
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on January 16, 2008, when we dismissed without opinion the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 22, 2006. The respondent presently requests that we reconsider our January 16, 2008, decision under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel.¿FN1º However, because we conclude that the respondent has failed to (1) establish that the 30-day filing deadline should be equitably tolled and (2) satisfactorily comply with the procedural requirements by which motions to reconsider under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed, the respondent’s motion will be denied. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In addition, the respondent’s request for a stay of removal will be correspondingly denied as moot. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(f).
 
Turning first to the equitable tolling issue, the respondent filed the presumptively time-barred motion to reconsider (accompanied by the motion for stay of removal) on March 14, 2008. However, the respondent argues that he meets the legal requirements necessary to equitably toll the 30-day filing deadline by which his otherwise untimely motion to reconsider is barred. See, e.g., Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999) (applying equitable tolling doctrine to 90-day filing period for motions to reopen where alien is prevented by his legal representative’s fraud from filing motion timely). We disagree.
 
In Lopez v. INS, supra., the Ninth Circuit recognized that the 90-day filing deadline applicable to motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where the respondent establishes that he has been prevented from meeting the 90-day deadline by “fraudulent legal representation” of which he remains ignorant “without any fault or want of diligence or care on his part.” Lopez v. INS, supra., at 1100 (citation and internal quotation marks omitted). In this case, the respondent contends that his former counsel’s statement in a letter dated January 18, 2008, that our decision dated January 16, 2008, is “final” and that he is “not eligible to appeal” our decision rises to the level of fraudulent legal representation (Respondent’s Mot. To Recon. Exh. A).
 
We conclude that former counsel’s statements do not constitute fraudulent legal representation because they were correct as a matter of law when they were made. First, our decision dated January 16, 2008, is a final administrative order or removal. See section 242(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(a). Second, the respondent’s former counsel was correct in advising the respondent that he was “not eligible to appeal” our decision or, in actual fact, the Immigration Judge’s decision dated August 22, 2006, because the Act has expressly foreclosed from judicial review discretionary determinations, such as the one rendered by the Immigration Judge (and affirmed without further opinion by us) denying the respondent’s request for relief from removal under former section 212(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (1994), on discretionary grounds. See section 242(a)(2)(B)(ii), (C) of the Act; Fernandez v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 592, 596 (9th Cir. 2006). Thus, we conclude that the respondent’s motion for reconsideration is time-barred and not subject to equitable tolling.
 
*2 Even were the respondent’s motion for reconsideration timely filed, the motion also fails to comply with the procedural requirements by which motions to reconsider under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed.¿FN2º In Matter of Lozada, supra, we set forth the elementary procedural requirements for a facially cognizable motion to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The first among them is the requirement that “the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard.” Id. at 637 (emphasis added).
 
In this case, the respondent’s declaration attached to the motion to reconsider fails to describe with sufficient detail the “agreement that was entered into with counsel.” Specifically, the respondent’s declaration only reflects that the respondent retained his former counsel in October of 2004 to represent him during the removal proceedings before the Immigration Judge, and that she “represented ¿the respondentº in an appeal before the Board of Immigration Appeals (Respondent’s Mot. To Recon. Exh. B).”¿FN3º Thus, the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel’s representation of him before us extended beyond filing the Notice of Appeal, submitting a brief or written statement in accordance with the established briefing schedule, and apprising the respondent of the issuance of our decision, all of which the respondent’s former counsel evidently did in accordance with their agreement. The respondent, in short, has failed to establish that the agreement he entered into with his former counsel provided for any legal representation after our adjudication of the respondent’s appeal. Further, we note that former counsel’s letter to the respondent dated January 18, 2008, invited the respondent to contact former counsel in the event he wished to make any further inquiries of his case (Respondent’s Mot. To Recon. Exh. A). The respondent evidently declined the invitation to do so (Respondent’s Mot. To Recon. Exh. B). Accordingly, we conclude that the respondent has failed to establish that his former counsel acted ineffectively.¿FN4º Thus, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion for reconsideration is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s request for stay of removal is denied as moot.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

In evident acknowledgment of his motion to reconsider’s untimeliness, the respondent simultaneously filed a motion to reopen the record of proceedings on March 14, 2008. However, the purpose of a motion to reopen is to state new facts to be proven at a prospective hearing date based on previously unavailable or undiscoverable evidence that indicates a meritorious claim for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) (2008); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 895 (9th Cir. 2003). Here the respondent argues that the intervening decision published by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, Camins v. Gonzales, 500 F.3d 872 (9th Cir. 2007), has rendered our decision dated January 16, 2008, legally erroneous. Thus, the respondent requests reconsideration of our earlier decision, not reopening for the purpose of submitting new, previously unavailable evidence or new applications for relief from removal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied because the respondent seeks reconsideration of our prior decision in light of an alleged change in the law subsequent to the issuance of the final administrative order of removal in this case. Further, the respondent’s motion to reopen is also defective due to its failure to comply with the basic regulatory requirement that the motion be supported by new, previously unavailable evidence or new applications for relief. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).

2

We are mindful that the Ninth Circuit has provided for a limited number of circumstances under which it will not respect our strict adherence to the procedural prerequisites for a motion to reopen or reconsider under a theory of ineffective assistance of counsel set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F.3d 934, 938 (9th Cir. 2003) (excusing alien’s failure to lodge a complaint with the appropriate state disciplinary authorities where alien offered a reasonable excuse for this omission and there was no suggestion of collusion between the alien and former counsel); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir. 2002) (dispensing with the Lozada requirements where counsel’s ineffective assistance was obvious and undisputed on the face of the record); Ontiveros-Lopez v. INS, 213 F.3d 1121, 1124-25 (9th Cir. 2000) (concluding that “arbitrary application” of the Lozada requirement is not warranted if alien shows “diligent efforts” to comply were unsuccessful due to factors beyond his control). However, we do not find that any of the circumstances requiring relaxation of the Lozada requirements within the territorial jurisdiction of the Ninth Circuit is present in this case. It is fundamental to establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel that the respondent show that his former counsel’s conduct ran afoul of an agreement for specific legal representation, and the respondent has failed to satisfactorily make such a showing. That is, the respondent has not provided sufficient evidence to establish that the filing of either post-decision motions with us after we issued the final administrative order of removal on January 16, 2008, or a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit was within the scope of the respondent’s agreement with his former counsel.

3

We observe, with respect to the insufficiency of the respondent’s evidence in this regard, that the respondent has failed to either (1) support the motion for reconsideration with a copy of the fee agreement(s) he entered into with his former counsel or (2) provide any explanation for his inability to do so.

4

We note in closing that Camins v. Gonzales, supra, was published on August 28, 2007, over six months after the respondent, acting through his former counsel, filed the appellate brief in this case. In the absence of evidence showing that the parameters of the agreement entered into between the respondent and his former counsel included the filing of supplemental briefs, post-decision motions, or a petition for review to the Ninth Circuit, however, we are unable to conclude and unwilling to presume that it did.


2008 WL 3861940 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 2938473 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMON ARCADIO GUTIERREZ CASTILLO
File: AXX XX5 617 - Boston, MA
July 7, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John E. MacDonald, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on April 22, 2008, on a motion to reconsider, at which time we vacated our prior November 30, 2007, order denying the respondent’s motion to reopen for ineffective assistance of counsel, and found that while the respondent was technically eligible for a waiver of inadmissibility pursuant to section 212(h) of the Act on a nunc pro tunc basis, the respondent still had failed to demonstrate a sufficient basis to afford the respondent nunc pro tunc relief Accordingly, we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our previous denial of his motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider our April 22, 2008, decision, arguing that we erred in considering issues of fact which should have been properly remanded to the Immigration Court. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is barred by regulation. A party may not seek reconsideration of a decision which denied a previous motion to reconsider. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(2). Herein, the respondent requests that we reconsider our previous April 22, 2008, denial of his motion to reconsider our November 30, 2007, decision. However, due to the unique posture of the case, in which we vacated, in part, the November 30, 2007, decision, we will also address the merits of his decision. In short, the respondent argues that the Board erred in our previous decision by finding that he was entitled to consideration of his waiver of inadmissibility on a nunc pro tunc basis. Specifically, we held that, in accordance with Matter of Balao, 20 I&N Dec. 440 (BIA 1992), the respondent had not demonstrated that he had departed and returned to the United States since the time of his 1980 conviction; moreover, we found that his delay in receiving a grant of his Form I-130 petition was not an error justifying nunc pro tunc consideration. See generally Fierro v. Reno, 217 F.3d 1 (1st Cir. 2000). The respondent now argues that he did, indeed, depart the United States subsequent to his 1980 conviction, and he further argues that the delay in the adjudication of his Form I-130 is a finding of fact which should be addressed, in the first instance, by the Immigration Judge.
 
Generally, a motion to reopen will not be granted unless it states new and material facts, is supported by affidavits or other evidentiary material, and establishes a prima facie case of statutory AXX XXX XXX and discretionary eligibility for the underlying substantive relief sought. See INS v. Doherty, 502 U.S. 314 (1992); Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992); 8 CF.R. §§ 1003.2 and 1003.8. In the case of claims under the standards set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), an alien must demonstrate at least prima facie evidence that the outcome of the case would have been different if he had been represented by adequate counsel. We find, in the instant case, that the respondent simply did not demonstrate prima facie eligibility for relief so as to demonstrate prejudice in his initial November 30, 2007, ineffective assistance of counsel claim. The respondent now argues that he had no idea that his departure and reentry into the United States was “relevant” until our April 22, 2008, decision; however, it is he who raised the issue of nunc pro tunc availability and should have been aware of the law set forth in Matter of Balao, supra, and Matter of Parodi, 17 I&N Dec. 608 (BIA 1980). Similarly, the respondent did not present a modicum of evidence that error or bad faith existed in the timing of his Form I-130 approval, but argues only that this issue should be explored at the Immigration Court level. Inasmuch as the respondent carries the burden of demonstrating that a motion to reopen is warranted, we find no error in our previous decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reconsider. Accordingly, inasmuch as the respondent does not identify specific error in our prior order, or call our attention to a change of law stated or relied upon in our earlier decision, the motion is denied. See 8 CF.R. § 1003.2; see Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991).
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 2938452 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARWAN HACHEM, BENEFICIARY OF A VISA PETITION FILED BY TIFFANY SHARNELL JACKSON, PETITIONER
File: AXX XX6 587 - Santa Ana, CA
July 2, 2008
 
IN VISA PETITION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER:
 
Joshua Effron, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Cathy Lin
Associate Regional Counsel
*1 The petitioner has appealed from a February 28, 2007, District Director’s (hereinafter, “director”) decision denying the visa petition she submitted on behalf of the beneficiary as her spouse. The District Director cited the lack of evidence the petitioner and beneficiary had shared a joint life in denying the petition. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
In visa petition proceedings, the petitioner has the burden of establishing eligibility for the benefit sought. Matter of Brantigan, 11 I&N Dec. 493 (BIA 1966). The petitioner must prove the required elements by a preponderance of the evidence. Matter of Pazandeh, 19 I&N Dec. 884 (BIA 1989). Where the bona fides of a marriage are challenged, the petitioner must present documentary or testimonial evidence to show that it was not entered into for the primary purpose of evading the immigration laws. See Matter of Phillis, 15 I&N Dec. 385 (BIA 1975). However, we note that the failure to produce affirmative evidence of the bona fides of the marriage, by itself, is not sufficient to establish that the marriage is a sham marriage. Compare 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(B), (D) with 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(ii).
 
In the Notice of Intent to Deny the director noted that at a preliminary interview on December 1, 2004, the beneficiary denied ever residing at the address listed on all immigration documents provided in conjunction with the instant visa petition as the primary residence of himself and the petitioner. Concerns were further raised when further investigation revealed that this residence was the same as that involved in a number of cases involved in an immigration fraud scheme related to the parties’ prior attorney. Various issues were noted at a site visit to the residence they allegedly shared, including evidence the petitioner had only recently moved into the apartment, the lack of pictures of them as a couple, the continued lack of joint assets, and the beneficiary’s “superficial knowledge” of the petitioner’s employment history. The notice concluded the parties had not lived together as a married couple, but had attempted to deceive the immigration service in order to obtain an immigration benefit by entering into a marriage that is not bona fide. The petitioner offered an extensive rebuttal to the notice.
 
The director’s decision denying the visa petition found the petitioner’s rebuttal arguments included incorrect assertions regarding statements provided by the beneficiary to immigration officers, and failed to provide a convincing explanation of concerns raised during a site visit by immigration officers. On appeal the petitioner alleges the site visit violated her due process rights, immigration officers had no right to speak with her daughter during the visit as she was not present, neighbors should have been contacted at that time but were not, maintains she and the beneficiary did not need to reside together to establish eligibility for adjustment for him, and offers further arguments regarding concerns raised by the site visit, among other allegations.
 
*2 We agree that the petitioner’s explanations for the numerous concerns raised in this record are not persuasive. In order to establish whether immigration benefits are to be made available based upon a marriage, it must be determined whether the bride and groom intended to establish a life together at the time they were married. Matter of Laureano, 19 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 1983); Matter of McKee, 17 I&N Dec. 332 (BIA 1980), The conduct of the parties before and after marriage is relevant to their intent at the time of marriage. Lutwak v. United States, 344 U.S. 604 (1953). Where, as here, there is reason to doubt the validity of the marital relationship, the petitioner must present evidence to show that the marriage was not entered into for the purpose of evading the immigration laws. Matter of Laureano, supra; Matter of Phillis, 15 I&N Dec. 385 (BIA 1975). Absent evidence that a marriage was a sham in its inception or entered into for the purpose of evading the immigration laws, a spousal visa petition cannot be denied merely because the parties were separated or no longer together, rather such is only a relevant factor in ascertaining the parties’ intent at the time of the marriage. Matter of Adalatkhah, 17 I&N Dec. 404 (BIA 1980); Matter of McKee, supra.
 
The beneficiary and petitioner both conceded that the address listed as their primary residence on all their initial documentation was, in fact, an address at which neither ever resided, but which instead was their former representative’s address. While the petitioner claims the parties were the “victims” of this individual (who was reportedly convicted of immigration fraud in the context of other immigration claims), there is no evidence the petitioner has attempted to contact this former representative and notify him of the allegations against him. As such, there is not even minimal evidence of compliance with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1stCir. 1988), and we give lessened weight to the petitioner’s claimed ignorance of the fraudulent methods of this individual.
 
Most significant in this record is the absence of evidence the parties entered into their marriage intending to establish a life together. See Matter of McKee, supra. In her rebuttal to the Notice of Intent to Deny and on appeal the petitioner fails to clearly explain exactly when and where she and the beneficiary resided together as a married couple. She vaguely excuses the absence of credible evidence in this regard by suggesting that they have an unconventional marriage. The sole address where she reports living with the beneficiary for an extended period is 1549 Westgate Avenue, Los Angeles. However, the record contradicts this claim, and fails to support her essential claim that her marriage is a bona fide one.
 
*3 The marriage certificate reflects the parties married May 10, 2003, and were both residing at 1107 W. 59th Street, Los Angeles. (However, an immigration investigation revealed that the “mailing address” listed in that document was attributable to the petitioner’s prior representative. The petitioner did not report this fact, and has not rebutted this finding.) In his corrected biography, the beneficiary does not report ever living at this residence. However, a photocopy of the petitioner’s license indicates she reported residing at this address in March of 2002, and a handwritten notation on that document indicates it is her sister’s home. We note that the petitioner sought to excuse her sister’s lack of knowledge in 2006 about “where we lived and when we resided together” and her husband’s then-current address by claiming her sister had “never had much contact” with the beneficiary (Response, pg. 5). The inference, then, is that the beneficiary never resided with the petitioner and her sister at the 59th Street address, nor is there any contrary evidence in this regard.
 
Rather, the record is devoid of any credible evidence regarding where the parties resided when they were married or shortly thereafter. The beneficiary’s biographic information form indicates he resided at 1325 Amhurst Avenue in Los Angeles from December of 2002 through December of 2003. The record contains no evidence regarding that address for either party whatsoever. A handwritten notation on that document notes, moreover, that he reported his address as 10959 Wellworth Avenue at the time of his NSEERS registration on January 8, 2003. As with the Amhurst Avenue address, there is no evidence of either party residing at this address.
 
Contrary to the petitioner’s suggestion otherwise on appeal, it is not sufficient that the parties resided together at some time during the course of their marriage to establish the bona fides of that relationship such that immigration benefits should be awarded. Rather, our inquiry is focused on their intent at the time they married, and the record supports a finding that the petitioner failed to meet her burden of proof in this regard. As noted, the conduct of the parties before and after their marriage is relevant this inquiry. The evidence regarding the claimed residence of the petitioner and beneficiary at 1242 Wellesley Avenue is highly inconsistent. The District Director noted that immigration officer’s conducted a site visit at that location and noted the closets were empty of women’s clothing, the petitioner’s unpacked suitcases were in a room with an empty closet, and it appeared the petitioner and beneficiary had slept apart the prior evening. The apartment was also devoid of any photographs of the parties as a couple.
 
The petitioner has offered evolving and inconsistent explanations for the officers’ observations. In her response to the Notice of Intent to Deny, the petitioner concedes her husband informed the officers he had moved into the Wellesley Avenue address a “few days” prior to the site visit, but failed to include the crucial assertion that he had been living there before that time with his parents (Response at 2). We observe the petitioner later explained the lack of pictures on the walls and the absence of any of her clothing in the closets by stating the beneficiary informed immigration officers “he had just moved in and that he was not pretending to have lived there for a long time” (Response at 3). This contradicts her report that he had, in fact, been living there for an extended period of time prior to his parents moving out.
 
*4 No explanation was offered as to why the beneficiary did not inform officers he had resided in the apartment for an extended time prior to the petitioner’s moving in. Moreover, the petitioner has not explained why she also did not reside there with her husband. She claimed she “frequently” visited there and spent the night, but has not overcome the fact that the apartment contained no photos of her or the couple, and the lack of any indication at the site visit that she kept clothing in the residence other than that contained in her luggage. Moreover, her generalized allegations fail to overcome the fact that the beneficiary informed the immigration officer that he, the beneficiary, and her daughter had lived together at that residence until his parents moved to the United States in mid-2006 (Decision at 2).
 
Similarly, the director correctly noted that the petitioner’s claim that there were no pictures of her and beneficiary as a couple, because he had “just” moved in, was contradicted by her alternative report that he had been living there for months by the time of the site visit and, in fact, she herself had allegedly resided there at one point (Response at 3). As with the majority of the petitioner’s claims regarding her and the beneficiary’s residences, she offered vague and largely uncorroborated assertions about when the residency occurred. She claimed the beneficiary informed the officers that he had “several pictures” and that “most things were still in boxes,” and claimed he subsequently hung up pictures. In contrast, the petitioner claimed on appeal that there were no photos of them together at all the pictures had been moved to the beneficiary’s parent’s new home (Notice of Appeal, pg. 5). She also argued that they “had little reason” to put new pictures on the walls as they intended to vacate the premises shortly thereafter. These allegations are clearly inconsistent. The petitioner offered similarly confused and contradictory explanations for why her clothes were in her suitcase at the apartment rather than put away. On appeal she indicated she was too busy to empty her suitcases, although she allegedly had moved in several days earlier (Notice of Appeal, pgs. 3-4). However, in her response to the Notice of Intent to Deny, she alleged that she, in fact, had “frequently” stayed at the apartment prior to moving in and, in fact, had some clothes in one of the closets, although the immigration officers did not observe this. The record contains no objective evidence whatsoever indicating she ever resided at this address.
 
The petitioner’s inconsistent claims included her explanation for why the beneficiary stated she had only been employed once during the entirety of their marriage when, in fact, she had held several jobs. Her explanation that her other jobs were of such short duration that he did not deem them worth mentioning is belied by the beneficiary’s specific report that she had “no other” employment during the marriage other than working as a telemarketer, which was also of short duration (Response at 5).
 
*5 The only address at which the petitioner allegedly did reside with the beneficiary as a married couple, by the petitioner’s own account, was at 1549 Westgate Avenue (Response at 3-4). The beneficiary’s biographic information reports he began living lived there in January of 2004. It is unclear when the petitioner allegedly resided there since, as with the remainder of her claims, she has offered no clear information in this regard. She reportedly left for Las Vegas in January of 2005, without the beneficiary, although it is unclear where she was living at that time (Response at 5). The only objective evidence of a joint residence at that address are phone and utility bills to the petitioner and beneficiary from November and December of 2004. We note these records, without more, are insufficient to establish they resided there together during that time period, particularly in light of the petitioner’s evolving and unsupported claims regarding their relationship.
 
Thus, by the petitioner’s own account and in light of the evidence of record it appears she and the beneficiary, at best, resided together for a short period, more than a year after their marriage. We agree in light of the sparse documentary evidence of record and the petitioner’s changing claims that she failed to establish the bona fides of the marriage, and has not overcome the concerns raised by the director. In this regard, we find no support for the petitioner’s claim the site visit was a violation of her due process rights. The concerns raised therein were identified in the Notice of Intent to Deny, and the petitioner was afforded an opportunity to respond to the same. Her failure to adequately do so does not constitute a due process violation. We similarly find no merit to her contention that the immigration officers who visited her home inappropriately spoke to her daughter. The daughter’s stepfather, the beneficiary, was present at the time this occurred and any comments made by the daughter have not been used against the petitioner. Finally, in closing we observe that while any financial problems on the part of the petitioner may explain the beneficiary wishing to keep his assets separate from hers, it fails to explain the lack of other evidence they held themselves out as married. Such evidence could include insurance policies, property leases, or other evidence regarding their courtship, wedding ceremony, and shared experiences. Matter of Phillis, 15 I&N Dec. 385 (BIA 1975). In sum, we find no merit to the petitioner’s allegations of error, and concur with the director that the petitioner failed to prove the bona fides of the marriage.
 
The petitioner may file a new visa petition on the beneficiary’s behalf that is supported by competent evidence that the beneficiary is entitled to the status sought under the immigration laws. See 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(D).
 
*6 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2938452 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 2938428 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMSON ENCILA
File: AXX XX5 634 - Los Angeles, CA
June 30, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Colin T. Greene, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Adjustment of status; voluntary departure; remand
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of the Philippines, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s decision of May 11, 2007, finding him removable as charged and granting him 60 days voluntary departure. During the pendency of the appeal, in lieu of a brief, the respondent filed a motion to remand seeking to reapply for adjustment of status based on his marriage on June 24, 2002 to a lawful permanent resident who subsequently naturalized on August 28, 2003. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) timely filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3). The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed, and his motion to remand will be denied.
 
In his Notice of Appeal (EOIR-26), the respondent asserts that “¿tºhere were miscommunications and/or mistranslations during the interview process since the questions of the Immigration Officer conducting the interview were vague.” To the extent that the respondent is referring to the interview conducted by U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services (USCIS) on March 11, 2005, which formed the basis for a Notice of Intent to Deny (NOID) issued on December 28, 2005, and the subsequent decision to deny the visa petition filed on behalf of the respondent on January 13, 2006, when USCIS received no response to its NOID, we note that the respondent’s appeal is untimely and otherwise improperly filed. An appeal from a USCIS decision must be taken within 30 days of the service of that decision by filing a Notice of Appeal (EOIR-29) directly with the USCIS office having administrative control over the record of proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(a)(2). In addition, only the party affected by a decision is entitled to appeal to the Board. See Matter of Sano, 19 I&N Dec. 299 (BIA 1985); Matter of DaBaase, 16 I&N Dec. 720 (BIA 1979); Matter of Kurys, 11 I&N Dec. 315 (BIA 1965). In this case, an appeal from the USCIS decision by the respondent’s spouse would have been due at the USCIS Los Angeles District Office on or before February 13, 2006. However, the respondent (rather than his spouse) filed an EOIR-26 (rather than an EOIR-29) with the Board (rather than the USCIS Los Angeles District Office) on June 11, 2007, approximately 16 months later.
 
*2 The respondent also argues in the EOIR-26 that “¿tºhe former legal representative did not provide us (as the new lawyer) the complete records for this case.” According to the respondent, his new counsel was “not given the opportunity to review and/or examine this case completely.” To the extent that the respondent is making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we note that he failed to comply with any of the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). We recognize that the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, has held that the Lozada requirements need not be rigidly enforced. However, we are not persuaded to dispense with the Lozada requirements entirely in cases such as this one, where ineffective assistance of counsel is not obvious and undisputed on the face of the record. See, e.g., Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). Moreover, the respondent failed to establish how he was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), The respondent makes no argument on appeal as to how the outcome of the proceedings might have been different if his new attorneys had had an “opportunity to review and/or examine this case completely.”
 
Finally, the respondent argues in the EOIR-26 that “the Immigration Judge did not give us the opportunity to explain the situation of our client.” It is well-established that under the Fifth Amendment, aliens are entitled to due process of law in removal proceedings. Reno v. Flores, 113 S. Ct. 1439 (1993); Yamataya v. Fisher, 189 U.S. 86 (1903). In this case, we find the respondent’s due process argument to be wholly without merit (Tr. at 32-62).
 
Turning to the respondent’s motion, the requirements for a motion to remand are essentially the same as those for a motion to reopen. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). A motion to reopen proceedings for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application and all supporting documentation. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). In this case, the respondent failed to submit a copy of his application of adjustment of status along with his motion to reopen. In addition, the respondent failed to submit the required affidavit of support under section 213A of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1183a. We note that the record contains an affidavit of support executed on December 31, 2003. However, not only is it now more than 4 years old, it reflects that the respondent’s wife, who has been unemployed or retired since 1941, has no income or assets. In order for an intending immigrant to overcome the public charge ground of inadmissibility, his or her sponsor must demonstrate the means to maintain an annual income of at least 125 percent of the Federal poverty line. See 8 C.F.R. § 213a.2(c)(2). In addition, the respondent failed to submit the required medical examination report along with his motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.5.
 
*3 Finally, even if the respondent had submitted the appropriate application and all supporting documentation, he failed to satisfy the requirements set forth in Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). In particular, the respondent failed to produce clear and convincing evidence indicating a substantial likelihood that his marriage to a woman 41 years his senior is bona fide. Evidence establishing that the marriage was entered into in good faith and not for the purpose of procuring an immigration benefit includes: (1) documentation showing joint ownership of property, (2) a lease showing joint tenancy of a common residence, (3) documentation showing commingling of financial resources, (3) a birth certificate of a child or children born to the petitioner and the beneficiary, and (4) affidavits of third parties having knowledge of the bona fides of the marital relationship. See 8 C.F.R. 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(B); see also Matter of Laureano, 19 I&N Dec. 1 (BIA 1993). In this case, the respondent produced no such evidence. Finally, as noted above, the DHS opposes the respondent’s motion for reasons unrelated to Matter of Arthur, 20 I&N Dec. 475 (BIA 1992).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed, and his motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2938428 (BIA)

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUZ LEMUS-CRUZ
File: AXX XX0 012 - San Diego, CA
June 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
K. Kerry Yianilos, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Lucinda Love
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Remand
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the June 21, 2007, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to terminate proceedings based on her claim of United States citizenship and ordering her removal to Mexico.
 
The respondent filed both a timely appeal and a motion to reopen removal proceedings. Having reviewed these documents, we will construe the latter as a motion to remand the record to the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(4); Matter of Oparah, 23 I&N Dec. 1, 2 (BIA 2000). The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has filed a brief responding to the appellate arguments but not to the motion.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge erred in finding her removable, as she claims that her attorney’s admission that she was born in Mexico was the only evidence offered of her alienage, and was legally insufficient. She further claims that in making such admission, prior counsel was “not fully aware of the facts” and “did not have a grasp on his client’s situation at all.” Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 5-6. We find these arguments to be entirely undermined by the fact that the respondent admits twice in the same brief that she was born in Mexico. Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 1, 5. Nevertheless, it has long been established that an admission by the respondent (or his or her counsel) of birth abroad is sufficient to establish a presumption of alienage and to shift the burden of proof to the respondent. See Murphy v. INS, 54 F. 3d 605, 609 (9th Cir. 1995); Matter of Benitez, 19 I&N Dec. 173 (BIA 1984); Matter of Castro, 16 I&N Dec. 81 (BIA 1976); Matter of A-M-, 7 I&N Dec. 332 (BIA 1956).
 
The respondent also claims on appeal that prior counsel “failed to adequately represent her case” to the extent that she was denied due process of law. Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 4. This claim of ineffective assistance of counsel will be dismissed, as the respondent has failed to substantially comply with the requirements for such a claim as set out in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that the Lozada requirements need not always be rigidly applied, and that substantial compliance may AXX XXX XXX suffice, particularly where ineffective assistance has been clearly demonstrated, efforts at compliance have been documented, and prejudice has been shown. See Lo v. Ashcroft, 341 F. 3d 934 (9th Cir. 2003); Melkonian v. Ashcroft, 320 F. 3d 1061 (9th Cir. 2003); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F. 3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). However, in the present case, the respondent has not shown any effort to comply with Lozada, She has not detailed her agreement with prior counsel, or established that the claimed failures were the fault of prior counsel. In the absence of such evidence, we find the claims as to what efforts prior counsel did or did not undertake to be speculative.
 
*2 We additionally find the respondent’s assertion that the Immigration Judge denied the respondent of due process by rushing to a conclusion on the issue of the respondent’s alienage to be unfounded. Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 4, 6. The record reveals, to the contrary, that the Immigration Judge discussed the issue at each hearing with prior counsel; advised counsel against admitting the respondent’s Mexican citizenship (Tr. at 7-8), advised the respondent to file an application for a certificate of citizenship on Form N-600 (Tr. at 11), and granted adjournments totaling sixteen months before proceeding with the merits hearing.¿FN1º
 
We next turn to the respondent’s motion to remand. In her motion, she has established a new fact that could not have been discovered at the time of the hearing. She was married on September 7, 2007 to a United States citizen. She claims that this new fact would now make her statutorily eligible for the relief of cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(l) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 229b(b)(l).¿FN2º Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 1.
 
The respondent has included with her motion an application for relief on Form EOIR-42B, and has attached supporting documentation. We find that the respondent has satisfied the requirements of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). See Matter of Yewondwosen, 21 I&N Dec. 1025 (BIA 1997). In its brief, DHS has not expressed opposition to the motion to remand. We will thus remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings to determine eligibility for such relief. We therefore need not address the remainder of the respondent’s arguments on appeal.
 
Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

2

In his oral decision, the Immigration Judge stated “the respondent is ineligible for cancellation of removal under Section 240A(b) of the Act because she does not have the qualifying relative” (I.J.at 5).

2008 WL 2782986 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WALTER AMILCAR BATRES-AREVALO
File: AXX XX7 422 - Chicago, IL
June 24, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raymond J. Sanders, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Alexandra Kostich
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, appeals the Immigration Judge’s April 26, 2007, decision granting him voluntary departure in lieu of removal under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a). On appeal, the respondent alleges that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during the course of his removal proceedings and that the Immigration Judge deprived him of due process by discouraging him from seeking asylum under section 208 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158.¿FN1º The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) has filed a brief in opposition to the appeal, claiming that the respondent pursued the instant appeal as a dilatory tactic and should, hence, no longer be able to pursue voluntary departure. In the alternative, the DHS asks us to remand the case to the Immigration Judge to impose a new departure bond that is separate from the cash appearance bond that was paid to ensure the respondent’s appearance in the Immigration Court throughout the proceedings. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Initially, we note that the respondent’s appeal is properly before us because the respondent never waived his right to appeal in accordance with the applicable regulations governing pre-hearing voluntary departure (Tr. at 22-24). See Matter of Ocampo-Ugalde, 22 I&N Dec. 1301, 1302-05 (BIA 2000) (holding that an appeal of a denial of cancellation of removal was properly before the Board from an order granting pre-hearing voluntary departure under the Act, where the alien never expressly waived his right to appeal in accordance with 8 C.F.R. § 240.26(b)(1)(i)(D), the predecessor to 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D)). Nevertheless, the respondent’s appeal is lacking in merit. We find no merit in the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims. Initially, we note that the respondent has failed to comply with any of the procedural requirements listed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for claims alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. See Patel v. Gonzales, 496 F.3d 829, 831-32 (7th Cir. 2007) (finding no abuse of discretion in the denial of a motion to reopen alleging ineffective assistance of counsel based on noncompliance with Lozada factors). Even so, we cannot say that counsel’s alleged errors impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing. See Mojsilovic v. INS, 156 F.3d 743, 748 (7th Cir. 1998). Specifically, the respondent does not allege with any specificity what “poor advice” his counsel afforded him (Form EOIR-26). Further, we cannot conclude that his counsel’s failure to advise him of his time frame for obtaining residency if his United States citizen father sponsored him for a visa adversely affected the respondent. The respondent testified at the hearing that his father had never helped him in this regard or even filed any petitions on his behalf (Tr. at 21), and he has not made any showing on appeal that his situation has changed.
 
*2 Similarly, the respondent’s claims that the Immigration Judge deprived him of due process are lacking in merit. Given the respondent’s failure to demonstrate that he is eligible for an exemption to the 1-year time bar for seeking asylum, or that he is entitled to asylum on the merits, we cannot conclude that the Immigration Judge’s alleged failure to permit the respondent to seek asylum deprived him of a fair hearing or otherwise deprived him of due process.
 
For the foregoing reasons, the respondent’s appeal lacks merit and would ordinarily be subject to dismissal, Nonetheless, as we have already discussed, the Immigration Judge did not have authority to grant the respondent voluntary departure in lieu of removal since the respondent did not waive appeal on all issues (Tr. at 22-24). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D); Matter of Ocampo, supra, at 1304-05. Hence, although the respondent does not appeal the grant of voluntary departure, we cannot reinstate his order of pre-hearing voluntary departure. Instead, we will remand the case to the Immigration Judge to allow the respondent to reapply for voluntary departure in accordance with the requirements set forth in the applicable regulations.¿FN2º On remand, the respondent is not precluded from seeking any other form of relief to which he may be entitled.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s April 26, 2007, order is vacated, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We note that these allegations were raised in the respondent’s Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26). On January 11, 2008, we denied the respondent’s motion to accept an untimely brief.

2

In light of our decision to remand this case, we need not address, at this time, the DHS’s contention that the Immigration Judge should impose a voluntary departure bond in this case that is separate from the appearance bond that was posted on the respondent’s behalf. We do note, however, that voluntary departure bonds are only required when post-hearing voluntary departure is granted; they are merely permissive when pre-hearing voluntary departure is granted. Compare 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(3) with 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(c)(3). In any event, we will allow the Immigration Judge to address the DHS’s contention in the first instance on remand.

2008 WL 2783028 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIO ERNESTO HUEZO-LANDAVERDE
File: AXX XX8 060 - Los Angeles, CA
June 19, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. Sec.212(a)(5)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(5)(A)(i)º - No valid labor certification
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This case is presently before the Board pursuant to an order issued by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The order remanded the record to the Board to reconsider the respondent’s motion under Sembiring v. Gonzales, 499 F.3d 981 (9th Cir. 2007) and Salta v. INS, 314 F.3d 1076 (9th Cir. 2002). However, the court rejected the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel contention, finding that the Board acted within its discretion in determining that he failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
In reviewing our decision dated October 31, 2006, and the evidence of record, we find that the Board erred in dismissing the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen. We find that the proceedings must be remanded pursuant to Salta v. INS, supra, wherein the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals determined that a sworn affidavit from the alien that neither she nor a responsible party residing at her address received the notice should ordinarily be sufficient to rebut the presumption of delivery and entitle an alien to an evidentiary hearing to consider the veracity of her allegations.¿FN1º Id. at 1079. Furthermore, in Sembiring v. Gonzales, supra, the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals determined that the alien affirmatively sought asylum, indicating that she did not have a motive to avoid immigration proceedings; she appeared in the Immigration Court on her originally scheduled hearing date; and her letter seeking reopening included documentation that supported her claim of lack of receipt of the notice rescheduling her hearing for an earlier date. Id.
 
As in Salta v. INS, supra, there is no indication that the respondent had any motive to avoid the hearing. The respondent appeared for his initial hearing before the Immigration Judge, and he had been a lawful permanent resident in the United States for many years. He also indicated that he contacted the Seattle immigration office numerous times to determine his status and to renew his alien registration card. The respondent submitted an affidavit with his motion to reopen and a declaration on appeal, stating that he moved to Seattle in September or October of 1999, and that he had mailed a change of address form to the Immigration Court in 1999. He also submitted the declaration of his aunt, Maria Henriquez, stating that in the fall of 1999, the respondent came to live with her for about three years. See Hernandez-Ortiz v. INS, 777 F.2d 509, 514 (9th Cir. 1985) (the Board must accept as true the factual statements contained in the affidavits submitted with a motion to reopen); see also Maroufi v. INS, 772 F.2d 597, 600 (9th Cir. 1985) (the Board is required to accept as true the facts stated in an alien’s affidavit in ruling upon a motion to reopen unless it finds those facts to be “inherently unbelievable”).
 
*2 Moreover, the record reflects that the Notice to Appear was served on the respondent by certified mail on June 17, 1997, but it was not filed with the Los Angeles Immigration Court until January 25, 2000.¿FN2º Consequently, if the respondent did file a change of address form with the Immigration Court in 1999, the court would not have had any record of the respondent, nor presumably any file in which to place the change of address form. Based on the facts of this case and the evidence submitted, we find that the respondent has presented sufficient evidence to overcome the presumption of effective service.
 
Finally, we note that the record indicates that the respondent was removed from the United States in December 2006. However, the Ninth Circuit held that where the removal proceedings are completed, and the alien is removed, but then reenters the United States unlawfully, he is not prohibited from filing a motion to reopen. Lin v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 979, 982 (9th Cir. 2007); see also Reynoso-Cisneros v. Gonzales, 491 F.3d 1001 (9th Cir. 2007). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The Board’s October 31, 2006, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s July 28, 2006, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals also noted that “the use of regular mail is now permitted by the governing statute, and that some of the Grijalva-Arrieta proof requirements (e.g. documentary evidence from the Postal Service, third party affidavits indicating improper delivery, etc.), which made perfect sense in connection with certified mail, clearly have no application under a regular mail regime.” Id. at 1080.

2

On appeal, the respondent submitted a copy of the Department of Homeland Serurity’s “Request to Reschedule NTA Interactive Scheduling System,” dated January 7, 2000, indicating that the case was scheduled to be heard on August 25, 1997, but the NTA was not timely filed with the Office of the Immigration Judge. See Respondent’s Appeal Brief, Attachment B.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: BASIM SHABA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Murray D. Hilts, Esquire
*1 This case was last before the Board on February 13, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen. On March 14, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent claims that he has presented sufficient evidence to establish that due to ineffective assistance rendered by his former counsel, Robert W. DeKelaita, Esquire, he did not receive the notice of hearing, and therefore, he failed to appear for his hearing on August 3, 2006. After the respondent was ordered removed in absentia, Mr. DeKelaita filed a motion to reopen and a Notice of Appeal on behalf of the respondent. The respondent has submitted numerous documents with his motion in support of his claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, including his affidavit, evidence that the respondent has filed a complaint against Mr. DeKelaita with the State Bar of Illinois, and evidence that he has informed Mr. DeKelaita of the allegations of ineffective asisstance of counsel. See Sako v. Gonzales, 434 F.3d 857 (6th Cir. 2006) (a motion to reopen before the Board due to ineffective assistance of counsel must first present the facts required by Lozada); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988).
 
In light of the respondent’s new allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel concerning his former attorney, Mr. DeKelaita, who filed the first motion to reopen, we find that the record must be remanded to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen and a determination as to whether the respondent has established that he failed to appear for his hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3). Accordingly, the record will be remanded.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Roger A. Pauley

2008 WL 2783064 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PATRICIA LAZARO
File: AXX XX2 641 - Miami, FL
June 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brad Alexander, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Motion to Reopen
 
*1 The respondent, a native and citizen of Peru, has made an untimely motion to reopen proceedings that were administratively final on January 15, 2004.¿FN1º The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent’s motion, which was received on April 29, 2008, was filed more than 18 months after the Board’s September 19, 2006, decision in this case and is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R, § 1003.2(c)(2). Moreover, the respondent has not demonstrated that she qualifies for any exception to the time limitation on motions to reopen. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1129a(c)(7)(C)(ii); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(changed country conditions or changed circumstances arising in the country of nationality).
 
However, to the extent that neither our prior decision, nor the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen adequately considered the fact that the respondent was never advised of the civil penalty for failure to voluntarily depart pursuant to section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d),¿FN2º and never considered the respondent’s assertion regarding ineffective assistance of counsel, we find it necessary to sua sponte reopen proceedings. See Tr. at 5-9; May 23, 2006, Statement of Robert A. Pajor and Patricia E. Pajor, at 2 (noting failure of prior attorney to timely file motion to reopen, and fact that prior attorney incorrectly filed motion to reopen). We therefore vacate our decision dated September 19, 2006, and the Immigration Judge’s decision dated July 3, 2006, and remand this record to the Immigration Judge for a new decision on the merits of the respondent’s June 12, 2006, motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997) (holding that the Board’s power to reopen proceedings on its own motion is limited to exceptional circumstances and is not meant to cure filing defects or circumvent the regulations, where enforcing them might result in hardship).
 
At that time, the Immigration Judge should specifically determine whether the respondent has established due diligence in making her motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel, and if so, whether equitable tolling is available to her. See, e.g., Rodas v. U.S. Attorney General 2008 WL 2122416 (11th Cir. 2008), quoting Abdi v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 430 F.3d 1148, 1150 (11th Cir.2005) (the statutory 90-day period for filing a motion to reopen is “mandatory and jurisdictional, and, therefore, it is not subject to equitable tolling”). If the Immigration Judge determines that equitable tolling is available, he should then consider whether the respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). In addition, the Immigration Judge should consider what impact the failure to comply with the statutory requirements for voluntary departure orders had on the respondent, and specifically, whether the respondent is precluded from adjustment of status as a result of the Immigration Judge’s failure to give such warnings.¿FN3º Finally, the Immigration Judge should consider whether any other circumstances in this case would warrant sua sponte reopening despite the time bar. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s September 19, 2006, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s July 3, 2006, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER; The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s motion to reopen in accordance with this decision, and for the entry of a new decision. Both parties may supplement the record, if necessary.
 
Frederick D. Hess

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PEDRO VALDIVIA-TOSTADO
File: AXX XX9 212 - Reno, NV
June 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven P. Brazelton, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen and reissue our decision dated March 2, 2006. In our order, we dismissed the appeal of an Immigration Judge order denying a request for a continuance and ordering the respondent removed. The motion is untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The respondent requests reissuance of our decision to allow a petition for review to be timely filed in the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. The respondent argues that, as former counsel acknowledge they failed to timely file a petition for review at the Ninth Circuit by filing the currently pending petition late, ineffective assistance of counsel has been established warranting reopening. See e.g. Dearinger v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042 (9th Cir. 2000). The motion includes some evidence of compliance with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in that counsel has admitted the late filing in a declaration and also sent a letter to “self-report” the untimely filing to the State Bar of Nevada (see Motion at Exhs. E, F). See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
However, the motion does not establish a plausible grounds for relief that would support a finding of substantial prejudice by the former counsel’s incompetence. Jie Lin v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 1014, 1027 (9th Cir. 2004). The respondent’s claim is the same as that adjudicated on appeal, i.e., that the Immigration Judge should have granted what would essentially have been an indefinite continuance to wait until the petition (Form I-130) filed on the respondent’s behalf is current. While a continuance may be granted for good cause, as the respondent is not eligible for relief, no good cause had been shown to warrant a continuance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.6. The priority date for the respondent’s approved petition is January 25, 1996, and, as a fourth preference family-sponsored visa for an alien from Mexico, that category was not current at the time of our decision, and is still not current.¿FN1º
 
The motion does not establish there was ineffective assistance that substantially prejudiced the respondent during the removal proceedings over which we have jurisdiction. See Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241 (9th Cir. 2000); Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968 (9th Cir. 2004). The motion is untimely and does not establish an exception. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

The Visa Bulletin for June 2008 indicates that visas are available for this category only for priority dates of December 15, 1994, or earlier.

2008 WL 2517567 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SEETA GUZMAN A.K.A. SEETA PRESAUD
File: AXX XX0 128 - New York, NY
June 5, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nadine A. Brown, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evalyn Douchy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on April 14, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia order of removal. On November 13, 2007, the respondent filed this motion to reopen our decision to enable her to apply for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion. As the motion has been filed more than 90 days after our previous decision, it is untimely. See section 240(c)(7)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(7)(C)(i); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks reopening based on allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel against the attorneys who previously represented her in these proceedings. The 90-day deadline imposed on motions to reopen may be equitably tolled where the motion is based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Jin Bo Zhao v. INS, 452 F.3d 154, 160 (2d Cir. 2006). In order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, however, an applicant must comply with the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In addition, an applicant seeking to toll the 90-day filing deadline must show that she exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period of time for which she seeks tolling. Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000). In the instant motion, the respondent has utterly failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements. Furthermore, it is not apparent from the evidence now before us that the respondent exercised due diligence in this matter. In this regard, we note that the respondent has not explained how her former attorneys’ alleged misconduct resulted in the untimeliness of the instant motion, and she has not described any measures that she took leading up to the filing of this untimely motion. We find, therefore, that equitable tolling based on the claims of ineffective assistance of counsel is not warranted in this case.¿FN1º Accordingly, the respondent’s untimely motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

We find no evidence of any exceptional situation which would warrant reopening of the respondent’s case on our own motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132, 1133-34 (BIA 1999) (“As a general matter, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions regulations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations”).

2008 WL 2400974 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: INGA MICHELLE DEWAR
File: AXX XX2 038 - Miami, FL
May 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George E. Crimarco, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated February 13, 2008. The motion is unopposed. Our review of allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are governed by our analysis in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Matter of Lozada, we stated that a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the applicant setting forth in detail the agreement between the applicant and his legal representative and what actions were to be taken on his behalf, (2) that the counsel whose reputation is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) an indication of whether a complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. In order to succeed in a claim of ineffective assistance of prior counsel, a respondent must also demonstrate that she suffered prejudice as a result. See Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
The respondent maintains that prior counsel, in preparing the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal pursuant to section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), failed to (i) inform the respondent of the types of evidence that would be necessary to meet the statutory requirement that she demonstrate 7 years’ continuous residence in the United States after having been admitted in any status, and (ii) direct the respondent to gather evidence, and ensure the appearance of her witnesses to testify, in support of her claim that she merited cancellation of removal as a matter of discretion. See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998). However, when the Immigration Judge and respondent’s counsel discussed the possibility that the respondent had not accumulated sufficient evidence to meet her burden of proof, the respondent stated in court that she had not failed to cooperate with counsel through inattention or design but instead because she was embarrassed to visit his office without the means to pay for his services (Tr. at 18-19). The record contains no response from prior counsel as to the respondent’s current claim of ineffective assistance. On the current record, it is not clear to what extent the respondent’s lack of cooperation, and to what extent prior counsel’s failure to prepare the respondent, were responsible for the dearth of evidence presented before the Immigration Judge. We therefore will remand the record with instructions that the Immigration Judge conduct the appropriate fact-finding to determine whether the respondent has met the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada.
 
*2 If, after resolving the necessary factual and legal arguments, the Immigration Judge determines that the respondent’s claims as to ineffective assistance are without merit, the Immigration Judge should once again enter an order of removal. If, however, the Immigration Judge determines that the respondent’s claims as to ineffective assistance have merit, the Immigration Judge should then entertain any applications for relief from removal.
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2400974 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: PEDRO ESTRADA-GOMEZ

APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
German T. Flores, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Matthew R. Hall
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Suspension of deportation; fair hearing; remand
 
*1 The respondent has filed a timely appeal from a decision, dated March 14, 2007, in which an Immigration Judge denied his application for suspension of deportation for failure to satisfy the extreme hardship and good moral character requirements for such relief.¿FN1º See former section 244 (a)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1254(a)(1). On January 3, 2008, the respondent filed a motion to remand the record to the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(4). The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
Initially, we will consider the respondent’s motion to remand. The respondent argues that the record should be remanded because the transcript “is replete with indiscernible statements.” See Respondent’s Motion at 16. While it is true that the transcript of the proceedings includes certain indiscernible lines of testimony, the respondent has not specified how such indiscernible testimony might have affected the outcome of this case. Specifically, he has not identified what material facts, if any, were omitted from the record. We find, therefore, that he has failed to show that such indiscernible lines of testimony caused him prejudice. See Matter of Exame, 18 I&N Dec. 303 (BIA 1982). Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to remand will be denied.
 
With regard to the respondent’s appeal, we first find that the transcript of the proceedings is sufficiently clear to judge the merits of the appeal. Second, we find that on appeal the respondent has not contested the Immigration Judge’s findings of fact or identified any factual errors underlying the Immigration Judge’s decision. Third, we find that the Immigration Judge properly applied the appropriate legal standards and correctly concluded that the respondent failed to establish eligibility for suspension of deportation on both good moral character and extreme hardship grounds. In sum, we find no reason to alter the Immigration Judge’s decision. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent also moves for reconsideration of our December 4, 2007, decision denying a previous motion to accept a late-filed appeal brief. The respondent argues that the failure to file the brief in a timely manner was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). He has not shown, however, that he suffered prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. See Hickman v. Spears, 160 F.3d 1269, 1273 (10th Cir. 1998) (explaining that to prevail on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, a petitioner must show, inter alia, that the deficient performance was prejudicial). Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is denied.

IN RE: JORGE RIVERA-HERNANDEZ A.K.A. JORGE RIVERA A.K.A. JOERGE RIVERA
