May 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Fatma E. Marouf, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reissuance
 
*1 On January 25, 2008, the respondent filed this motion to reissue our December 14, 2004, dismissal of his appeal from an Immigration Judge’s July 22, 2003, decision. The respondent claims that the office of his former attorney first informed him of our decision on December 9, 2007. See Tab A (Respondent’s Declaration), at 4. The respondent now requests that we reopen and reissue our decision to enable him to file a timely petition for judicial review. We will grant the motion.
 
Pursuant to the regulations, the Board is obligated to give notice of its decisions by serving them, physically or by mail, upon the parties affected. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.1(f) and 1003.13. According to the record, the Board mailed its decision dismissing the appeal to the respondent’s former attorney. The respondent claims after the appeal was filed, he repeatedly contacted his former attorney to inquire into the status of his case. See Tab at 3. He further claims that he immediately contacted his former attorney’s office after learning on or about December 8, 2007, that he was required to report before December 14, 2007, to the nearest Immigration and Customs Enforcement office. See Tab A at 4; see also Tab P (Notice to Obligor to Deliver Alien). On December 9, 2007, the office of the respondent’s former attorney informed the respondent that his appeal had been dismissed. See Tab A at 4. The respondent then sought the counsel of his current attorney, and he obtained a copy of his file from his former attorney’s office. Id. at 4-5. In the file, the respondent found a letter, addressed to this Board, in which the respondent’s former attorney states that “¿nºeither our office nor the client is in receipt of the decision notice.” Id. at 5. The respondent has proffered a copy of that letter, dated May 22, 2006, and date-stamped by the Board on May 30, 2006, in which the respondent’s former attorney requested a copy of our December 14, 2004, decision. See Tab Q.
 
Given the particular circumstances of this case, we find it appropriate to reissue our decision dismissing the appeal, thereby enabling the respondent to petition for judicial review. First, the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim before the Board. See Tabs A and T. See also Iturribarria v. I.N.S., 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Second, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has explained that when alien is prevented from filing an appeal in immigration proceeding due to counsel’s error, and is thereby deprived of appellate proceeding entirely, error mandates a presumption of prejudice inasmuch as the adversary process itself has been rendered presumptively unreliable. See Dearinger ex rel. Volkova v. Reno, 232 F.3d 1042, 1045 (9th Cir. 2000). In this case, although the Board properly mailed its decision to the respondent’s former attorney, the May 22, 2006, letter confirms that the respondent was not notified of our decision prior to the deadline for filing a petition for judicial review. Furthermore, in his sworn declaration, the respondent has stated that despite repeated inquiries, his former attorney did not inform him of our decision prior to December 9, 2007. See generally Singh v. Gonzales, 494 F.3d 1170, 1173 (9th Cir. 2007) (remanding a case to the Board to consider and specifically address the effect of affidavits of the alien and his attorney). Accordingly, we will reopen the proceedings on our own motion, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and vacate our December 14, 2004, order. A final order in this matter will be issued as of this date, incorporating by reference the text of the attached vacated order.
 
*2 ORDER: The proceedings are reopened, the respondent’s motion to reissue is granted, and our December 14, 2004, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated December 14, 2004, attached hereto, is hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2401013 (BIA)

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LILIA ANHEL GONZALEZ-MUNIZ
File: AXX XX4 926 - Los Angeles, CA
May 23, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gabriella Navarro-Busch, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Prepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening; cancellation of removal; voluntary departure
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated March 21, 2007, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider and reopen these proceedings so that she may pursue an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1). The respondent, through counsel, has appealed. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s appeal.
 
With regards to the respondent’s motion to reopen, the Ninth Circuit has held that the failure to comply with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), will not bar consideration of a motion based on the ineffective assistance of counsel, where the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance. See Granados-Oseguera v. Gonzales, 464 F.3d 993, 998 (9th Cir. 2006). The factors set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are intended to ensure both that an adequate factual record exists for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (finding that ineffectiveness assistance was clear on the face of the record where counsel failed to file application for relief).
 
In this case, the respondent’s counsel was ineffective because she failed to timely file an application for cancellation of removal. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 900 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding ineffective assistance of counsel when counsel failed to file application for cancellation of removal by date imposed by Immigration Judge); Rodriguez-Lariz, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226 (9th Cir. 2002)(finding ineffective assistance of counsel when counsel failed to timely file application for suspension of deportation). The respondent was presumptively prejudiced by counsel’s deficient performance because it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings. See Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9th Cir. 2004). In this regard, the respondent’s motion includes an application for cancellation of removal and supporting documentation.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is sustained, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge so that the respondent may be afforded an opportunity to apply for cancellation of removal. At the reopened proceedings, both parties may present additional evidence on the issue of the respondent’s eligibility for relief from removal, and the Immigration Judge shall enter a new decision.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2401078 (BIA)
End of Document

CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(I)º -Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(G)ii, I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(G)(ii)º - Marriage Fraud - failed or refused to fulfill marital agreement
 
  
APPLICATION: Reconsider, reopen, remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on April 22, 2008, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s September 27, 2007, denial of her motion to reconsider.¿FN1º On April 28, 2008, the respondent filed what appears to be a motion to reconsider captioned “motion to withdraw the former attorney’s motion to reconsider improvidently filed and consider our motion to reopen filed on November 2007 that has not been reviewed.” The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has not filed a response to the respondent’s motion. The respondent’s motion to reconsider is granted and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
In her April 28, 2008, motion, the respondent alleges that she had filed a motion to reopen on November 17, 2007, based on ineffective assistance of counsel which the Board failed to consider in their April 22, 2008, decision.¿FN2º See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). On May 12, 2008, the respondent submitted additional documents which reveal that her former attorney had been issued an informal admonition by the Office of Bar Counsel in the District of Columbia on April 24, 2008, relating to her ineffective representation of the respondent before the Immigration Judge and the Board of Immigration Appeals. Based on our review of the record and the documents submitted by the respondent, we find it appropriate to reopen and remand proceedings to the Immigration Judge to allow the respondent the opportunity to apply for the relief that she seeks. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider is granted. Also, proceedings are reopened and the record remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
Frederick D. Hess

2008 WL 2401093 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: HAYDEN GAVIN EDWARDS
File: AXX XX9 005 - Bloomington, MN
May 21, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
H. Fitzmore Harris, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel J. Pornschloegl
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This proceeding has been before the Immigration Judge and the Board on numerous previous occasions. In November 2006, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed, and the respondent filed a timely Notice of Appeal. During the pendency of his appeal, the respondent submitted evidence that his conviction for attempted criminal sale of a controlled substance-crack cocaine-had been amended to attempted possession of a controlled substance. Based on the additional evidence submitted, on February 7, 2007, the Board remanded the record for further examination of the respondent’s removability and eligibility for cancellation of removal. During most of his initial proceeding before the Immigration Judge, as well as during the first appeal, the respondent was represented by Melvin Duke.
 
After the Board remanded proceedings, the respondent hired new counsel, Dewey Nelson, to represent him (Tr. dated 3/8/07, 3/15/07, and 3/22/07). In an oral decision issued March 22, 2007, the Immigration Judge found the respondent removable as charged under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(II), based on the amended conviction,¿FN1º and denied all relief. For purposes of the appeal, the respondent again retained Mr. Duke. On June 28, 2007, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his motion. Our June 28, 2007, decision was our final administrative decision for purposes of appeal. Thereafter, on August 15, 2007, and January 3, 2008, the Board denied two more motions.
 
On February 11, 2008, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen. The motion is untimely, as well as number-barred, and the respondent has not demonstrated that a regulatory exception applies. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2) and (3). The respondent alleges that the time and number limitations should be equitably tolled because Mr. Duke provided ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The fact that Mr. Duke was disbarred by the state of New York in 2000, and may not have been qualified to practice before the Immigration Courts or the Board, does not demonstrate per se ineffective assistance of counsel, or otherwise satisfy the requirements of equitable tolling, so as to to justify reopening sua sponte.¿FN2º
 
*2 To warrant equitable tolling, the respondent must show, inter alia, that he exercised due diligence during the period he seeks to have tolled. Habchy v. Gonzales, 471 F.3d 858 (8th Cir. 2006); Kanyi v. Gonzales, 406 F.3d 1087 (8th Cir. 2005). In the instant case, the respondent admits that he was concerned about Mr. Duke’s representation as of October 2006. The respondent was apparently so concerned about Mr. Duke’s representation that, after the Board remanded proceedings, he retained new counsel. He then, however, hired Mr. Duke again for purposes of filing another appeal, as well as filing motions to reopen. Since the respondent was on notice as early as October 2006 regarding Mr. Duke’s alleged ineffectiveness, and has not provided an adequate explanation, the respondent has not demonstrated due diligence in raising ineffective assistance of counsel at this late date.
 
Further, the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by Mr. Duke’s actions. The Immigration Judge held that the respondent was removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, which provides, with certain exceptions not applicable here, that an alien convicted of a violation of (or a conspiracy or attempt to violate) any law or regulation of a State relating to a controlled substance as defined in 21 U.S.C. § 802, is inadmissible (I.J. Dec. dated 3/22/07 at 4). The record indicates that the respondent was convicted of attempted criminal possession of a controlled substance in the fourth degree in violation of New York Penal Law § 220.09, Subsection 1 (Gp. Exh. 8, Tab A and Transcript of Plea Agreement). According to the transcript of the plea agreement, the narcotic drug involved was crack cocaine (Gp. Exh. 8, Transcript). See also Respondent’s Rebuttal filed 2/1/07, Exh. A). The respondent’s state conviction is a violation of a law relating to a controlled substance as defined in 21 U.S.C. 802. See 21 U.S.C. §§ 802(6) and (17); 21 U.S.C. §§ 812, Schedule II(a)(4); 21 U.S.C. § 846; NY. Penal Law, § 220.09 subs. 1; N.Y. Pub. Health Law § 3306, Schedule II(b)(4). It is clear, therefore, that the respondent was removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act.
 
The respondent contends that Mr. Duke should have contested removability because his certificate of disposition conviction documents indicate that he was convicted under New York Penal Law § 220.09.00, but § 220.09 does not include a subsection “00”, that the respondent did not admit to possessing crack cocaine, and the transcript of the plea agreement was not properly admitted. We disagree. The certificate of disposition indictment indicates that the respondent was convicted under § 220.09, not § 220.09.00. Further, the transcript of the plea agreement, which was admissible, clearly indicates that the respondent’s amended conviction was for crack cocaine under § 220.09 subsection 1 (Gp. Exh. 8, Transcript). See Shepard v. U.S., 544 U.S. 13, 16 (2005)(court may examine, inter alia, transcript of plea colloquy); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.41.¿FN3º The transcript of the plea agreement was marked as evidence, and treated as admitted since the Immigration Judge relied on it in reaching his decision, and neither Mr. Nelson nor the Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) objected.
 
*3 It is unclear, moreover, why current counsel is challenging the Immigration Judge’s reliance on the transcript of the plea agreement. Without it, the record of proceeding would include two convictions for controlled substances-a certificate of disposition conviction for attempted criminal possession, as well as for attempted criminal sale, because the certificate regarding the conviction for attempted criminal possession does not indicate that it amended the attempted criminal sale conviction. Moreover, the Federal Rules of Evidence do not apply in immigration proceedings, but to the extent such rules apply, the transcript’s authenticity cannot be questioned at this time because there is no question that the transcript of the plea agreement is what it was purported to be-the transcript of the respondent’s original plea and conviction for attempted criminal sale, his sentencing, and his amended conviction.¿FN4º Accordingly, the respondent has not demonstrated that he was prejudiced by Mr. Duke’s alleged ineffectiveness regarding removability.
 
Since the respondent was removable under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, he was not eligible to adjust his status because there was no waiver available for inadmissability under section 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(II) of the Act, except for simple possession of a small amount of marihuana, see section 212(h) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(h), and, accordingly, the respondent has not demonstrated prejudice. With regard to voluntary departure, the respondent was not eligible for such relief while the proceeding was pending before the Immigration Judge. See section 240B(b)(l)(B) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b)(1)(B); section 101(f)(3) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(f)(3). Further, at that time, he was not represented by Mr. Duke, and there is no claim of ineffective assistance pending against Mr. Nelson.
 
Regarding the respondent’s argument that Mr. Duke failed to pursue naturalization once the respondent became eligible, the respondent has also not demonstrated prejudice. It is fundamental that Immigration Judges and the Board do not have the authority to determine whether an alien in proceedings is prima facie eligible for naturalization for purposes of terminating proceedings under 8 C.F.R. § 1239.2(f). See Matter of Hidalgo, 24 I&N Dec. 103 (BIA 2007)(Because the Board of Immigration Appeals and the Immigration Judges lack jurisdiction to adjudicate applications for naturalization, removal proceedings may only be terminated pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1239.2(f) where the DHS has presented an affirmative communication attesting to an alien’s prima facie eligibility for naturalization.). See also Matter of Cruz, 15 I&N Dec. 236 (BIA 1975). An applicant bears the burden of demonstrating that he has been a person of good moral character, and the DHS may consider conduct and behavior beyond the statutorily prescribed period. 8 C.F.R. § 316.10. The respondent filed his application for naturalization with DHS on or about November 30, 2007. As of today’s date, the respondent has not presented evidence that he has obtained an affirmative communication attesting to his prima facie eligibility from the DHS, and, therefore, cannot demonstrate prejudice. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*4 Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAMUEL BARBOSA DA COSTA JEAN RAFAEL DA COSTA MICHELE ASSUMPCAO DA COSTA THIAGO ASSUMPCAO COSTA NOEULUIZ COSTA
Files: AXX XX2 205 - Miami, FL
AXX XX4 617
AXX XX4 618
AXX XX4 619
AXX XX4 620
May 20, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents, natives and citizens of Brazil, appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision of September 20, 2006. We will accept this appeal by certification pursuant to our authority in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(c). See generally Matter of Liadov, 23 I&N Dec. 999 (BIA 2006). However, the respondents* appeal will be dismissed on the merits.
 
As an initial matter, we note that the respondents have submitted new evidence on appeal, including a letter from the First Brazilian Baptist Church of South Florida dated October 2005, a pamphlet from the Community Blood Centers of South Florida (CBCSF) indicating that Elaine Hernandez, who is of unknown relation to the respondents, gave blood on October 24, 2006, and two printouts from a Florida Department of State website (www.sunbiz.org) relating to Paint by Manny and Prachel Painting and Waterproofing.
 
This Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984). Accordingly, we cannot consider any new evidence presented for the first time on appeal, and any party asserting that this Board cannot properly resolve an appeal without further fact-finding must file a motion to remand. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv). In this case, even if we construe the respondents’ submission as a motion to remand, the new evidence that the respondents seek to offer is either immaterial, as in the case of the CBCSF pamphlet, or was previously available and could have been discovered or presented at the former hearing on September 20, 2006, as in the case of the church letter and the printouts from www.sunbiz.org. See C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1); see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). Accordingly, we will adjudicate the appeal based upon the evidence contained in the record when it was before the Immigration Judge.
 
On appeal, the respondents argue that they received ineffective assistance of counsel from the seven attorneys who represented them at various junctures during the last 10 years. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this matter arises, in order to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must establish that his or her counsel’s performance was deficient to the point that it impinged upon the fundamental fairness of the hearing such that the alien was unable to reasonably present his or her case. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1273 (11th Cir.2005). In addition, the alien must establish that he or she suffered prejudice as a result of counsel’s ineffective assistance. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Prejudice exists when the performance of counsel is so inadequate that there is a reasonable probability that but for the attorney’s error, the outcome of the proceedings would have been different. See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., supra, at 1274.
 
*2 In this case, the respondents have not complied with any of Lozada’s procedural requirements. They have not provided detailed affidavits setting forth the agreements that they reportedly entered into with each of their seven attorneys. They have not informed the individuals whose integrity or competence is being impugned of the allegations leveled against them and given them an opportunity to respond. Nor have they indicated whether complaints have been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not. Although the respondents present a sympathetic case, they have failed entirely to comply with any of Lozada’s procedural requirements, which the Eleventh Circuit has determined are not unduly onerous. See Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222-23 (11th Cir.2003). Accordingly, the respondents’ appeal is dismissed, and their motion to remand is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHARLES NDUBUISI IGWENAGU A.K.A. CHARLES N. IGWEMAGUE
File: AXX XX1 639 - Miami, FL
May 20, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Zareefa Khan, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated April 1, 2008. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) has not filed any opposition to the respondent’s current motion, and we therefore deem the current motion to be unopposed. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g). The motion is denied.
 
A motion to reconsider requires an assertion by the moving party that the original decision be reexamined in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. See generally Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 404 (BIA 1991); 8C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Initially, we note that in support of his motion, the respondent has cited cases arising outside the Eleventh Circuit. However, this Board historically follows a court’s precedent in cases arising in that circuit. See Matter of Olivares, 23 I&N Dec. 148, 149 (BIA 2001), citing Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25, 31 (BIA 1989). Thus, the cases cited by the respondent arising outside the Eleventh Circuit are not controlling.
 
In the instant case, the respondent has failed to present additional legal arguments which could not have been made during our earlier review of this matter on April 1, 2008. Additionally, the respondent appears to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. However, a petitioner attempting to reopen or reconsider based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must at least “substantially” comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (setting forth the requirements a respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel). See Dakane v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 399 F.3d 1269, 1274 (11th Cir. 2005); see also Gbaya v. U.S. Att’y Gen., 342 F.3d 1219, 1222 (11th Cir. 2003). In the instant matter, the respondent has failed to do so. The respondent has not identified any change in the law affecting his appeal nor has he identified an aspect which we overlooked during our prior review on April 1, 2008. Having considered the respondent’s contentions, we are not persuaded to disturb our decision dated April 1, 2008, for the reasons stated therein.
 
*2 Molly Kendall Clark
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2401042 (BIA)

2008 WL 2401148 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERGIO SUAREZ DOMINGUEZ
File: AXX XX8 214 - San Antonio, TX
May 15, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Justin Adams
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Rescission
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed an Immigration Judge’s June 26, 2007, decision denying his motion to reopen the Immigration Judge’s April 9, 2007, decision ordering him removed in absentia. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) opposes the respondent’s appeal. The appeal is dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent avers the Immigration Judge failed to consider that he did not appear at his hearing as he was misled by his attorney (Notice of Appeal). He maintains former counsel failed to inform him that it was possible a pending motion to change venue could be denied, and the respondent was required to appear at his scheduled hearing (Resp.’s Br.). The respondent contends he had no reason to avoid his hearing, and asks that his case be reopened.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen was essentially based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. As the Immigration Judge correctly noted, the respondent did not comply with any of the requirements for such a claim, as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Such a motion should be supported by 1) an affidavit setting forth “in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken,” as well as any representations made by counsel to the alien; 2) proof that the movant has informed former counsel of the allegations in writing, as well as any response received; and 3) a statement detailing “whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not.” Id. As the respondent’s motion did not include any of the noted evidence, the Immigration Judge did not err in finding that reopening was not warranted.
 
For the respondent’s benefit, we further note it is well-established that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986). A respondent should not assume that a motion for a change of venue will be granted. Matter of Rivera, supra.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
*2 Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2401148 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 2401060 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: IHEANYI ONWUKA
File: AXX XX5 091 - Houston, TX
May 9, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda L. Walker, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated March 13, 2008. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent argues for the first time in an attachment to his motion for reconsideration that his failure to meet the 90-day filing period for submitting a motion to reopen was due to “wrong advice from ¿hisº previous lawyer.” He further states that he consulted the lawyer on July 17, 2007, 5 days after the Immigration Judge’s July 12 2007, decision, and was advised to “wait until ¿heº received another communication from the Immigration Office, which should arrive in about 90 days.”
 
“A motion to reconsider is a request that the Board reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked. A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard.” Matter of O-S-G, 24 I&N Dec. 56, 57 (BIA 2006) (citations omitted). To the extent that the respondent is raising a claim of ineffective assistance by his former counsel, he has failed to comply with any of the requirements for asserting such a claim outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).¿FN1º See Lara v. Trominiski, 216 F.3d 383, 389 (5th Cir. 2000) (general application of the Lozada requirements does not constitute an abuse of discretion by the Board). The respondent failed to submit an affidavit outlining in full his contacts with his prior attorney, and failed to provide his former counsel with an opportunity to respond. It is unclear what correspondence the respondent would have been waiting for after the Immigration Judge issued a final decision in this case, nor has the respondent indicated whether he was aware of, or was explained, the filing requirements for submitting a motion to reopen. Moreover, we find that the respondent could and should have addressed the timeliness issue during his appeal. While the respondent states in his motion for reconsideration, through counsel, that he just realized that an October 23, 2007, letter addressed to the Immigration Judge, which outlined his timeliness argument, was sent to the wrong address, he could and should have argued this issue in his appeal brief to the Board. We therefore find that the respondent has failed to establish a basis for equitable tolling of the statutory filing period. See Coleman v. Johnson, 148 F,3d 398, 402 (5th Cir. 1999) (“A garden variety claim of excusable neglect does not support equitable tolling.”); Davis v. Johnson, 158 D3d 806, 811 (5th Cir. 1998) (Equitable tolling is permitted only “in rare and exceptional circumstances.”). Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

Under Matter of Lozada, supra, a motion based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel should be supported by an affidavit, reflect whether a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, and include a response to the allegations by former counsel or report counsel’s failure or refusal to respond. Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

2008 WL 2401101 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDGAR LEONARDO HERNANDEZ
File: AXX XX8 133 - Arlington, VA
May 1, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David J. Rothwell, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marcia E. Gottesman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (not sustained)
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(ii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude
 
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 US.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (not sustained)
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (not sustained)
 
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony (not sustained)
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; remand
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of EI Salvador, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 30, 2007, decision. In that decision the Immigration Judge, inter alia, denied his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a), as a matter of discretion. The appeal will be sustained, and the record will be remanded.
 
The respondent alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel as, although the family told the former counsel that a diagnosis of a psychiatric disorder had been made and requested that the counsel obtain the respondent’s medical records, his former counsel failed to do so. It is asserted that respondent’s former counsel clearly did not understand the significance of these medical records as he told the family that these records would not be helpful. We note that in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), we laid out the requirements that a respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. First, the motion should be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent attesting to the relevant facts. Second, before the allegation is presented to the Board, the former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure to respond, should be submitted with the motion. Finally, if it is asserted that prior counsel’s handling of the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, the motion should reflect “whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation, and if not, why not.” Matter of Lozada, supra; see also Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997).We note that the United States Court of Appeal for the Fourth Circuit, the jurisdiction in which this matter arises, in Stewart v. INS, 181 F.3d 587, 596 (4th Cir. 1999), declined to consider an alien’s argument of ineffective assistance of counsel where the alien failed to provide an affidavit detailing his agreement with counsel, failed to inform counsel of the allegation against him or her and provide him or her with opportunity to respond, and failed to indicate whether the complaint had been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities.
 
*2 Here, the respondent, after delineating the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, indicated that affidavits have been submitted from the respondent’s brother and father attesting, on behalf of the respondent, to the relevant facts. In the brother’s affidavit, he indicated that a complaint had been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding such representation and the respondent’s current counsel related that he had attempted to contact respondent’s former counsel to advise him of the charges against him. The respondent’s father related that he was involved in retaining the former counsel to represent the respondent and paid some of the fees, but never saw a written contract. While the respondent has attempted to comply with the Lozada requirements, to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel at a deportation proceeding, an alien must show not only ineffective representation, but also prejudice to him which occurred as a result of that ineffectiveness. See Figeroa v. INS, 886 F.2d 76, 78 (4th Cir. 1989).
 
In the instant case the respondent has presented medical evidence indicating that in August 2007 he underwent a psychiatric evaluation, resulting in several psychiatric diagnoses, and that he had been receiving treatment for a period of time. He argues that his then current attorney failed to present this evidence at his removal hearing. The respondent’s brother has provided a statement detailing a conversation with the prior attorney describing the information the brother provided to the attorney, and the attorney’s judgment that the medical evidence would not help the respondent. After noting that he was at the removal hearing but was not asked to testify, the respondent’s brother detailed the information relevant to the respondent’s mental health he would have provided. The Board notes that, in closing arguments before the Immigration Judge, the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) pointed to the respondent’s contention that he had been diagnosed with a bipolar disorder which might explain his criminal history, but that there was no evidence in the record (Tr. at 136). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent’s claim of a bipolar disorder was not credible, noting the lack of corroboration, including the lack of evidence that he had ever being formally evaluated by a psychiatrist or psychologist (I.J. at 5). While the respondent’s attorney had indicated that he had attempted to talk to a physician at the facility where the respondent had been held (Tr. at 136), there is no indication that a written request for the respondent’s medical records was made.
 
We conclude that the respondent, by reason of his former counsel’s apparent failure to request and submit the respondent’s medical records into evidence, has demonstrated that he received ineffective assistance of counsel. Moreover, we find that the respondent was prejudiced, particularly in light of fact that this lack of corroboration caused the Immigration Judge to call the respondent’s credibility into question.
 
*3 Accordingly, the appeal will be sustained, and the removal proceedings will be remanded for further consideration of the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal, to include, if desired, the submission of additional evidence by the parties.
 
The following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2401101 (BIA)

2008 WL 2079335 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FITZGERALD ALPHONSO SANCHEZ
File: AXX XX4 772 - Los Angeles, CA
April 24, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Justin W. Chaney, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on January 24, 2008. The final administrative order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on March 26, 1992. The pending motion was filed well over a decade late. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues, alternatively, that the Board did not properly mail a copy of its 1992 decision to his prior counsel, or that his prior counsel failed to properly mail a copy of the decision to him. Inasmuch as the record indicates that the Board properly served its decision on the respondent’s attorney of record, we will address his alternative ineffective assistance of counsel argument. The respondent, through current counsel, claims that former counsel told him that “as long as he does not receive a bag and baggage letter then everything was ok”, and that he did not hear anything more until he was detained by DHS in 2007. He also indicates, in his brief, that his prior attorney was supposed to file an appeal with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. In support of his ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent has submitted, inter alia, a copy of a letter that he wrote to his former counsel indicating that he would be filing a complaint with the state bar if this Board determines that the 1992 decision was properly served; he further submits a letter from his prior counsel indicating that his office has no information on the respondent because office policy mandates that closed cases are held for seven years after which time all information in the case is shredded.
 
There is an inadequate basis for tolling the motion to reopen period in this case. See Valeriano v. Gonzales, 474 F.3d 669, 673 (9th Cir. 2007); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003) (individual seeking to rely upon “equitable tolling” of a filing deadline must show that he has acted with due diligence). Despite his claims against former counsel, the respondent fails to demonstrate why he waited so long to contact new counsel and file the pending motion. As the DHS states in its response, the respondent has not provided any reasonable explanation as to why he did not inquire about the status of his case with either his attorney or the Board during the past 15 years. Moreover, even were the motions deadline tolled in this case, the motion would be denied for failure to show prejudice resulting from the alleged failures of prior counsel. Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F. 3d 1182, 1189 (9th Cir. 2004); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&NDec. 553 (BIA2003); Matter of Lozada, 19I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). In order to show the required prejudice, the respondent must show “plausible grounds for relief.” Serrano v. Gonzales, 469 F.3d 1317,1319 (9th Cir. 2006). The respondent has failed to make this showing. The respondent had a full and fair hearing on his application for section 212(c) relief before the Immigration Judge, and the Board considered all the relevant factors before finding that the Immigration Judge had correctly concluded that a favorable exercise of discretion was unwarranted in the respondent’s case. The motion is, therefore, denied.
 
*2 Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 2079335 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 2079347 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MONGKHON SUTTHI ART A.K.A. MONGKHON SUTTHIART
File: AXX XX1 399 - Eloy, AZ
April 18, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kuyomars Golparvar
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION; Cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Thailand, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 9, 2008. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent was admitted to the United States as a lawful permanent resident on March 7, 1995. On October 26, 2006, the respondent was convicted in two separate cases (F04901939-9 and F06903726-8) for possession of methamphetamine in violation of section 11377(a) of the California Health and Safety Code.
 
We will affirm the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent is removable as charged under section 237 (a)(2)(B)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i), as an individual convicted of offenses relating to a controlled substance. The respondent conceded that he was removable on this basis at the hearing (Tr. 23). The charge is supported by conviction records which show that the respondent pleaded guilty to illegal possession of methamphetamine, as set forth above.
 
We have thoroughly reviewed of the transcript of proceedings and the evidentiary record. We are satisfied that the respondent received a full and fair hearing in this matter.
 
We find no merit in the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We note initially that the respondent has failed to comply in any measure with the procedural requirements for such claims set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (compliance with Lozada, supra, is generally required to assert claim of ineffective assistance of counsel). We find no evidence in the transcript of proceedings that the respondent’s former attorney, Ms. Monika Sud-Devaraj, who was substituted for the respondent’s initial counsel and represented him at his master calendar and merits hearings, was negligent in her representation. We find no indication that the respondent had any difficulty communicating with Ms. Sud-Devaraj, and we note that the court provided a Thai interpreter at the respondent’s merits hearing. The Board does not have the authority to review the respondent’s custody status in these proceedings, as bond and removal proceedings are separate and apart. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.19(d). However, we note that it would not have been improper for Ms. Sud-Devaraj to withdraw a pending request for bond because the respondent’s drug conviction made him subject to mandatory detention. See section 236(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1226(c). We do not find that any action or inaction of former counsel in this matter resulted in prejudice.¿FN1º See Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241, 1246 (9th Cir. 2000) (alien must show that the allegedly ineffective representation may have affected the outcome of the case); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 We are satisfied that the Immigration Judge thoroughly considered the record and properly weighed the adverse factors evidencing the respondent’s undesirability as a permanent resident against the social and humane considerations presented on behalf of the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal (I.J. at 4-8). See Matter of C-V-T-, 22 I&N Dec. 7 (BIA 1998). We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny this relief in the exercise of discretion.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

No prejudice resulted from the failure of the respondent’s initial counsel to appear for a master calendar hearing (Brief at 6). The Immigration Judge simply reset the hearing for another date.

2008 WL 1924626 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUAN TRANQUILINO PALOMARES-QUINTERO
FILE: AXX XX3 565 - SAN FRANCISCO, CA
April 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patricia Vargas, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kim B. Wong
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(A), I&N Act ¿ 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(A)º (withdrawn)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iiX I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(ii)º - Convicted of two or more crimes involving moral turpitude (withdrawn)
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime involving moral turpitude
 
Lodged: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(A), I&N Act ¿ 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(A)º
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on February 5, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the entry of a removal order. The Board had previously sustained the DHS’s appeal of the grant of section 212(c) relief. The respondent now moves to reopen to pursue adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition. The motion to reopen will be granted.
 
The respondent wants to pursue adjustment of status, and moves for reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel. A motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days after entry of the final administrative decision in the case. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent argues that this time period should be equitably tolled because he was unaware of his former counsel’s ineffectiveness until counsel realized in August 2007 that the respondent had been eligible for adjustment of status, and advised the respondent to consult with other counsel. The respondent did so, and new counsel filed this motion to reopen in September 2007, less than 90 days after the respondent learned of his former counsel’s error. Under these circumstances, we conclude that the respondent acted diligently and is entitled to equitable tolling of the time period for filing a motion to reopen. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 898 (9th Cir. 2003) (attorney misconduct may warrant equitable tolling until alien met with new counsel and discovered former counsel’s fraud).
 
Typically, to warrant reopening based on the ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must comply with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10(1st Cir. 1988), and demonstrate that he was prejudiced by counsel’s deficient performance. The Ninth Circuit has held that the failure to comply with the Lozada requirements will not bar consideration of the motion where ineffective assistance of counsel is clear from the record. Granados-Oseguera v. Gonzales, 464 F.3d 993, 998 (9th Cir. 2006). “Lozada is intended to ensure both that an adequate factual basis exists in the record for an ineffectiveness complaint and that the complaint is a legitimate and substantial one.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). The record of the proceedings themselves may be adequate to serve those functions. Id (holding that ineffectiveness was clear on the face of the record where counsel failed to file application for relief).
 
*2 Here, the record sufficiently reveals the ineffectiveness of the respondent’s former counsel in failing to seek adjustment of status. It is apparent from the record that the respondent is eligible for adjustment of status. His wife is a United States citi2en and has been throughout these proceedings. They were married long before the commencement of these proceedings. Although the respondent is removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i), for having been convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude, that conviction does not render him inadmissible because of the petty offense exception. Section 212(a)(2)(A)(ii)(II) of the Act, 8US.C. § 1182(aX2)(A)(ii)(II). The respondent’s crime was charged as a misdemeanor punishable by not more than 1 year of imprisonment, and he was sentenced to only 60 days in jail. See Cal. Penal Code § 264(a) (Deering 1991); Exh. 2. We are not persuaded by the DHS’s argument that the respondent’s conviction bars him from a favorable exercise of discretion. The exercise of discretion requires a balancing of all favorable and adverse factors.
 
The respondent thus appears prima facie eligible for adjustment of status. There is no tactical reason why counsel would have declined to pursue such relief. The Board’s August 30, 2005, order which found the respondent ineligible for section 212(c) relief remanded the record to the Immigration Judge “for further proceedings” including a removal order “as warranted.” It thus left open the possibility of pursuit of adjustment of status at that time.
 
Under these circumstances, counsel’s ineffectiveness is apparent on the record. Thus, under controlling Ninth Circuit law, the Lozada requirements are satisfied. Prejudice is also apparent on the record, as the respondent is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status and was prevented from pursuing that relief. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra, at 527 n. 12. The respondent has submitted an application for adjustment of status and supporting documentation along with his motion to reopen. We conclude that the respondent met his burden of showing that reopening is warranted. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be granted.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Edward R. Grant
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 1924626 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CONSUELO NAVARRO CHAVEZ
FILE: AXX XX6 057 - ATLANTA, GA
April 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mario Ninfo, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Removability; cancellation of removal; suspension of deportation
 
*1 The respondent has appealed an August 29, 2006, Immigration Judge’s order finding her removable, and ineligible for relief from removal. In doing so, the Immigration Judge noted the respondent had not filed for any relief. The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent admitted the truth of the factual allegations and charges of removability (Tr. at 16).¿FN1º On appeal she does not allege any error in this regard. Rather, she contends the Immigration Judge erred in finding her removable as an aggravated felon (Resp.’s Br. at 4-5). However, the Immigration Judge made no such finding. Rather, as noted, she admitted removability as charged under sections 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) and (II) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), (II) (Exh. 1). We similarly find no merit to the respondent’s generalized allegation that she was afforded ineffective assistance of counsel by an unidentified individual in an unknown manner (Notice of Appeal). The respondent has in no manner complied with the requirements enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) for such a claim. To the extent she contends counsel who represented her in her criminal proceedings provided her with ineffective assistance, such a claim is in the nature of a collateral attack on her conviction (Resp.’s Br. at 5-6). However, neither the Immigration Judge nor this Board can entertain a collateral attack on a judgment of conviction unless that judgment is void on its face, and cannot go behind the judicial record to determine the guilt or innocence of the respondent. See Matter of Madrigal-Calvo, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996); Matter of Fortis, 14 I&N Dec. 576, 577 (BIA 1974).
 
While the respondent suggests she should be permitted to seek suspension of deportation, such relief is not available to her as she is in removal proceedings. Section 240A of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b (Resp.’s Br. at 7-8). The respondent also avers she is eligible for cancellation of removal (Tr. at 7-8). However, she admittedly was convicted of manufacture/delivery of more than 2,000 but less than 5,000 grams of marijuana, and was sentenced to serve 60 days in jail and 24 months probation (Resp.’s Br. at 4; Exh. 1). Section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act defines an aggravated felony as “illicit trafficking in a controlled substance (as defined in section 102 of the Controlled Substance Act (“CSA”)), including a drug trafficking crime (as defined in section 924(c) of title 18, United States Code).” Illicit trafficking in a controlled substance is commonly defined as an unlawful trading or dealing in a controlled substance. See Matter of Davis, 20 I&N Dec. 536, 540-41 (BIA 1992). Further, section 924(c)(2) of Title 18 defines a drug trafficking crime as “any felony punishable under the CSA.” See Lopez v. Gonzales, 127 S.Ct. 625, 631 (2006). Pursuant to Lopez, supra, a state offense constitutes a “felony punishable under the ¿CSAº” only if it proscribes conduct punishable as a felony under that federal law. Id. at 633.
 
*2 The respondent’s conviction may therefore qualify as an aggravated felony under section 101 (a)(43)(B) of the Act, as the transfer of such quantity of marijuana is punishable as a felony under the CSA. See 21 U.S.C. §§ 802(8), (11) (including in the CSA definition of distribution “the actual, constructive, or attempted transfer of a controlled substance”), 812(c) (listing marijuana as a controlled substance), 841 (a)(1), (b)(1)(D) (punishing distribution of marijuana as a felony under the CSA). See also Lopez v. Gonzales, supra at 633 (analyzing the term “drug trafficking crime” included in section 101(a)(43)(B) of the Act, and holding “that a state offense constitutes a ‘felony punishable under the ¿CSAº’ only if it proscribes conduct punishable as a felony under that federal law”).
 
Consequently, the evidence of record “indicates” that a ground for mandatory denial of the respondent’s cancellation of removal application - specifically, a conviction for an aggravated felony - “may apply.” See generally 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). The respondent therefore bears the burden of establishing that her conviction for delivery of marijuana does not qualify as an aggravated felony, Id. The respondent has not submitted any evidence to meet this burden, and thus has failed to prove, by a preponderance of the evidence, that the mandatory ground of denial enumerated at section 240A(a)(3) of the Act does not bar her from obtaining cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act. Based on the foregoing, we find that the respondent has failed to establish statutory eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act, and we therefore find no support for the respondent’s contention she is eligible for cancellation of removal, and, further, no basis for remand of the instant proceedings.¿FN2º We recognize that the respondent has cited a variety of humanitarian factors that she wishes to have considered in our adjudication of her claim. However, while compelling, these factors relate to relief in the form of cancellation of removal, a form of relief for which she is ineligible.
 
In closing, we find no support for the respondent’s various allegations of constitutional infirmity in her removal proceedings (Resp.’s Br. at 4-7). We are not unsympathetic to the situation faced by the respondent. However, it is well-settled that this Board cannot entertain constitutional challenges to the statutes we administer. See Matter of Rodriguez-Carrillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1035 (BIA 1 999). In sum, we find no error by the Immigration Judge in finding the respondent ineligible for relief.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.¿FN3º
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Therefore, and in light of the fact that the respondent’s ineligibility for relief was established on the pleadings, we find no error by the immigration Judge in issuing a summary order. See Matter of A-P-, 22 I&N Dec. 468 (BIA 1999).

2

The issue of whether the DHS conclusively established that the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony is immaterial to our disposition of the instant appeal, as it is uncontested that the respondent is removable as charged, and the respondent bears the burden of proving that she has not been convicted of an aggravated felony for purposes of establishing eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act. As noted, that burden has not been met.

3

For the respondent’s benefit we note she appears to suggest on appeal that her conviction be vacated on the merits of the underlying criminal procedures, such as noncompliance with any associated requirement that a defendant be informed of the immigration consequences of the conviction, rather than simply for immigration purposes. See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003). Unless and until this occurs, her conviction is considered final for purposes of our review. However, should her conviction be thusly vacated, she should file a motion to reopen with this Board and, if the same is untimely filed, seek sua sponte reopening of the proceedings.

*1 APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent has filed a motion to reopen and remand the record of his removal proceedings to the Immigration Judge, so that he may pursue an application for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. He is married to a United States citizen who has filed a visa petition on his behalf. See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002) (stating that motion may be granted notwithstanding the pendency of the visa petition). The motion to reopen will be granted, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
Although this motion is untimely, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel previously and he has, substantially, complied with our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir 1988). See Vang v. Gonzales, 478 F.3d 133 (2d Cir. 2007). In this regard, we note that he had filed for a visa petition based on his current marriage previous to our final decision in this case. The brief previous to our final decision, however, only asked that we consider his appeal regarding a pending employment visa petition and one filed by his father. At that time, had the Board been properly notified, a remand would have been appropriate. See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002).
 
While the Board is concerned that well over 3 years have passed since the visa petition was filed and the respondent has submitted no indiction of its current status, we will, nonetheless, reopen the proceedings. We note that the Department of Homeland Security has not indicated any opposition to the current motion. At the reopened proceedings, it will be incumbent upon the respondent to demonstrate the current status of the petition and the likelihood of its success. The Immigration Judge may then consider the totality of the circumstances in deciding how best to proceed. The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings.

2008 WL 1924656 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KHACHATUR VARDAN KARAPETYAN A.K.A. KHACHATUR KARAPETYAN A.K.A. ARTHUR KARAPETYAN
FILE: AXX XX3 596 - LOS ANGELES, CA
April 8, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Houman Varzandeh, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Remand; section 212(c) waiver
 
*1 The respondent has filed an appeal from the decision of the Immigration Judge dated September 28, 2006, in which the Immigration Judge found the respondent subject to removal and further found that his applications for relief from removal were abandoned. The respondent has also moved to remand the record for further proceedings on the basis that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, the respondent claims that his former attorney, Rita Mahdessian, failed to timely file the appropriate application for relief from removal, falsely advised the respondent that she had complied with the filing and other requirements set forth by the Immigration Judge, and failed to advise the respondent that she was suspended from the practice of law at the time she initially provided him with legal counsel. We have no indication in the record before us that the Department of Homeland Security opposes the respondent’s motion to remand. The motion is therefore deemed unopposed. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(g)(3). The motion will be granted.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640.
 
The respondent has complied with the technical requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and his former attorney has not disputed his allegations. Additionally, the record suggests that the respondent is eligible to be considered for the relief he seeks, a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c) (Tr. at 9). Accordingly, we find the respondent has established ineffective assistance of counsel. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence); see also Morales Apolinar v. Mukasey, 514 F.3d 893 (9th Cir. 2008) (alien need not demonstrate she would win on claim absent deficient performance of counsel).
 
*2 Accordingly, we will remand the record for further proceedings on the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 1924656 (BIA)

2008 WL 1924585 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIETA KHACHATRYAN
FILE: AXX XX3 287 - HARTFORD, CT
April 3, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eugene Khavinson, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; Reopening
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Armenia. This matter was last before us on November 30, 2007, when we dismissed her appeal of the Immigration Judge’s April 21, 2006, decision ordering her removal. We found no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondent’s application for adjustment of status based on her failure to adduce clear and convincing evidence of the bona fides of her marriage to a United States citizen (I.J. at 2-3). See Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). We also found that the Immigration Judge did not err in refusing to accept such evidence on the day of the hearing or grant a continuance, as the respondent had over 3 months to submit documentation within 10 days of the hearing and failed to exercise due diligence (I.J. at 2). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. Presently before the Board is a motion to reconsider our decision and a separate motion to reopen based on based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The motions are denied.
 
A motion to reconsider must specify the asserted errors in the prior Board decision and be supported by relevant authority. Section 240(c)(6)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6)(C); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006) (discussing procedural requirements for motions to reconsider). A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision as defective in some respect, based on the previous factual record. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402-03 (BIA 1991). The filing of a motion to reconsider is not a process through which a party may submit, in essence, the same brief submitted on appeal and seek reconsideration by generally alleging error in the Board’s prior decision. Matter of O-S-G-, supra, at 58. The movant must specify the factual and legal issues raised on appeal that were erroneously decided or overlooked in our initial decision, or show how a change in the law materially affects the prior decision. Id.
 
Here, the respondent has not complied with these standards. To the extent that she has alleged general error in our prior decision, this is not a proper basis for a motion to reconsider. See id. The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge failed to comply with his duty to establish the record. See section 240(b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(1); Yang v. McElroy, 277 F.3d 158, 162 (2d Cir. 2002). However, we are not persuaded that we erroneously overlooked this issue in our prior decision, at which time the respondent was represented by her former counsel Alexander Lumelsky, Esquire. While an Immigration Judge has an obligation to establish the record, it is the respondent’s burden to establish her eligibility for the relief of adjustment of status. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(d). We reaffirm our holding that the Immigration Judge acted reasonably and within his discretion in affording the respondent 3 months to marshal the evidence necessary to support her claim, and denying a continuance when she failed to do so. The absence of any evidence of the bona fides of the respondent’s marriage was not a result of the Immigration Judge’s failure to develop the record, but of the respondent’s failure to exercise due diligence in satisfying her burden of proof. Therefore, the motion to reconsider is denied.
 
*2 Concerning the motion to reopen, a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), in which we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The claimant must also establish prejudice resulting from his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that the alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In this case, the respondent has not complied with the first prong of Matter of Lozada. While her supporting documentation contains two personal unsworn statements, neither of these is an “affidavit.” BLACK’S LAW DICTIONARY (8th ed. 2004). Furthermore, she has not set forth in detail the agreement entered into with Mr. Lumelsky concerning the actions to be taken and what representations he did or did not make in this regard. In fact, a letter from Mr. Lumelsky in the respondent’s documentation informs her that “¿aºs we discussed, you must provide me with evidence of your life together with your husband from the date of your marriage until the hearing,” and “if I do not receive any documentation from you and your husband by March 1, 2005, I will withdraw as your attorney.” The letter further instructs the respondent to file a motion with the Immigration Court with “your Philadelphia attorney” if she desired to have venue transferred to Philadelphia. These representations call into doubt the respondent’s assertion that Mr. Lumelsky told her that she could either bring the required evidence 10 days before the hearing or to the hearing itself. Moreover, the respondent has provided no evidence of prejudice, as required to prevail on the instant motion. The respondent has raised no argument concerning her eligibility for adjustment of status in her motions and the only evidence of record tending to establish the bona fides of her marriage was submitted by Mr. Lumelsky in connection with the appeal. As this evidence is outdated, it is insufficient to show a bona fide marriage and thus demonstrate prejudice. For these reasons, the motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel is denied.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motions are denied.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 1924585 (BIA)

2008 WL 1924543 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VIRGILIYA KRAVETS IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
FILE: AXX XX9 065 - BOSTON, MA
March 31, 2008
 
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Desmond P. FitzGerald, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
 
  
APPLICATION: Waiver under section 212(c)(4); remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals from the January 8, 2008, decision of the Immigration Judge denying her motion to reconsider. The Immigration Judge initially ordered the respondent removed on July 27, 2007, After the respondent failed to submit proof to the court that she had filed a Form 1-751, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent had abandoned any available relief and ordered the respondent removed to Lithuania.¿FN1º The respondent moved to reopen, arguing that her prior counsel had been ineffective in failing to timely file the Form 1-751. The Immigration Judge denied that motion on December 20, 2007, because the respondent failed to submit proof that she had filed the 1-751, had not met the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel set out in Mailer of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1998), and was not eligible to adjust her status based on her marriage to her current husband. The respondent then filed a motion to reconsider before the Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reconsider Immigration Judge. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reconsider on January 8, 2008, finding that she had not identified any defects in the prior decision. It is from that decision that the respondent now appeals.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that she did not abandon her application for relief, that she has met the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and that she is eligible to petition for a hardship waiver pursuant to section 216(c)(4) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1186a(c)(4). The DHS has not submitted a brief in this case.
 
A motion to reconsider requests that the original decision be reexamined in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(2) (2007) a (“motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s prior decision and shall be supported by pertinent authority”).
 
Preliminarily, the record establishes that the respondent has met the requirements set out in Matter of Lozada, supra, for ineffective assistance of counsel claims (Attachments to Respondent’s January 7, 2008 Motion to Reconsider). In addition, the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent could not adjust her status based on her current marriage to a United States citizen because she entered the United States on a fiance visa based on her engagement to her first husband. The Immigration and Nationality Act prohibits the adjustment of status from a fiance visa under section 101(a)(l 5)(K), except as a result of a marriage to the same the citizen who filed the fiance petition. Section 245(d) of the Act. The record does not contain evidence that the respondent entered the United States with a fiance visa, but if that is the case, then the Immigration Judge’s ruling regarding adjustment of status is correct.
 
*2 In her motions to reopen and to reconsider, however, the respondent does not seek adjustment of status through her current husband; instead, she seeks a hardship waiver to remove the conditional basis of her permanent resident status based on her marriage to her first United States citizen husband. See section 216(c)(4) of the Act. The Immigration Judge did not address this aspect of the respondent’s case in her December 20, 2007, decision. Accordingly, we find that a remand is appropriate for consideration of the respondent’s eligibility for any relief.
 
The record is remanded for further proceedings in accordance with this decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley
