** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PALWINDER GHOTRA A.K.A. HARSARAN KOCHHAR
FILE: AXX XX1 090 - BLOOMINGTON, MN
March 31, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Luisito Lopez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Terry M. Louie
Senior Litigation Counsel
 
CHARGE:
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on January 22, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen in absentia proceedings. In doing so, the Board found the respondent had received appropriate notice of his hearing. On December 14, 2007, the respondent submitted a “motion to reconsider,” asking the Board to sua sponte reopen proceedings as he avers he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is denied.
 
We initially note the respondent’s motion is incorrectly titled, as its true nature is one seeking reopening.¿FN1º A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based on the previous factual record, as opposed to a motion to reopen, which seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 403 (BIA 1991); see also section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) and Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Here, the respondent does not allege the Board committed error based on the record before us at the time of our prior decision. Rather, he contends prior counsel afforded him ineffective assistance by failing to include with his first motion to reopen the original envelope for his hearing notice, although he had given the same to her. With his motion he has provided evidence he did not previously submit, including affidavits from himself and a former landlady, and a March 5, 2007, letter from former counsel.
 
The respondent’s motion is time-barred as it was filed more than 90 days after the Board’s decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Moreover, as the Department of Homeland Security notes in its opposition, the respondent has not complied with any of the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). We appreciate that former counsel indicated in her letter that the respondent had “exhausted the appeals,” however without further inquiry it is unclear whether she referred to his available appeals within the administrative system, whether he had exhausted the level of appeals available pursuant to their representation agreement, or if she truly did not appreciate he could appeal to the federal circuit court. As the respondent did not inform former counsel of his allegations against her, she has had no opportunity to clarify what she meant in her letter. Former counsel’s mistaken referral to a “motion to reconsider” when discussing the requirements for a motion to reopen is also not per se evidence of ineffective assistance. As noted, current counsel has made a similar such mistake. Moreover, there is no indication that a motion to reconsider was supportable at that time as, to date, the respondent has not demonstrated the Board committed any error in our prior decision¿FN2º. Similarly, the respondent has not indicated he had informed Ms. Baquero he had any “additional information” which would have supported a motion to reopen. As such, although her reference to the nature of the motion was in error, he has not shown he had provided her with evidence supporting a second motion to reopen.¿FN3º Since the respondent has not informed Ms. Baquero of his allegations against her, he has not afforded her an opportunity to respond to his claim that he, in fact, provided her with the envelope bearing the hearing notice and she failed to file the same with his motion. Ms. Baquero’s letter makes no reference to this document. Without more, the respondent has not shown he was provided with ineffective assistance of counsel, and reopening is not warranted.¿FN4º
 
*2 In closing, we do not find that the facts presented represent exceptional circumstances warranting the exercise of our sua sponte authority to reopen. See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997) (Board retains limited discretionary power under the regulations to reopen or reconsider only in exceptional circumstances); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). In addition, we decline to exercise our discretionary authority to reopen as the record demonstrates the respondent was ordered excluded in 1995, and admitted he reentered the United States in 1996 without lawful authorization (Forms I-213, I-831). He also admitted departing and returning to this country several times thereafter, also without lawful authorization. Moreover, the record contains evidence indicating he was involved in the unlawful smuggling of two aliens into the United States (Forms I-213, I-831; Minn. Dept. of Public Safety, Field Report, 1/5/06). Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 1924544 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: THUY THI THANH NGUYEN
FILE: AXX XX1 563 - BLOOMINGTON, MN
March 31, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
David L. Wilson, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Darrin E. Hetfield
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on November 26, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision. The respondent has timely filed a motion to reopen the proceedings. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has indicated its opposition to reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
The respondent seeks reopening in order to allow her to present testimony from her first husband, Anthony Toan Ly, regarding the bona fides of their former marriage. The respondent claims that she received ineffective assistance from her former attorney, who she claims rebuffed her repeated suggestions that she should contact Mr. Ly to ask him to testify on her behalf. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent asserts that Mr. Ly’s testimony will validate her claim that she and Mr. Ly married for love, and not in order to circumvent the immigration laws, and that the termination of the marriage was through no fault of her own. The respondent has proffered affidavits from herself as well as Mr. Ly.
 
Pursuant to the Board’s holding in Matter of Lozada, supra, the respondent informed former counsel of the allegations made against him. In a letter written in response to the respondent’s allegations, former counsel states that he did in fact ask the respondent to provide Mr. Ly’s address and whereabouts to his office from the beginning of the proceeding. However, the respondent never provided the address but stated that Mr. Ly had gone to Canada with another woman.
 
We find that reopening is unwarranted. The respondent has not shown that she received ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent’s contention that former counsel rejected her suggestions to procure Mr. Ly’s testimony is unworthy of belief in view of the many inconsistencies between the respondent’s affidavit and that of Mr. Ly; the inconsistencies between the affidavits and the testimony given by the respondent under oath at the January 31, 2006, hearing; and former counsel’s statement that he had asked the respondent to provide Mr. Ly’s address, but she did not do so.
 
The respondent states in her affidavit that she told her attorney on at least two occasions prior to the January 2006 hearing that she believed Mr. Ly would be willing to appear in court to testify on her behalf. However, this conflicts with the respondent’s testimony that she does not want to contact Mr. Ly because she knows that he lives with another woman in Canada, and she does not think that he would come to court and explain what happened to their relationship if she did contact him (Tr. at 59). Additionally, the respondent’s statement in her affidavit that Mr. Ly’s mother had told her she had Mr. Ly’s telephone number after the respondent called her following the first master calendar hearing conflicts with the respondent’s testimony that she does not know where to find Mr. Ly and was only told that he lived with “that woman” in Canada when she telephoned his friend (Tr. at 58-59).
 
*2 Mr. Ly’s affidavit also conflicts with the respondent’s testimony as well as her affidavit. Mr. Ly states that he had contact with the respondent from time to time, and she had told him that “she had an interview and later that she had court”. He further states that he wrote a letter to the respondent’s former counsel and would have gone to her hearing, but the respondent explained that former counsel did not want her there. These statements are inconsistent both with the respondent’s January 31, 2006, testimony that she had not talked to Mr. Ly since December 1999 and did not know where to find him (Tr. at 58) and with the allegations in her affidavit that she was given Mr. Ly’s telephone number by his mother, and subsequently telephoned him and told him of her situation, only after the Immigration Judge entered his order granting her voluntary departure.
 
The letter from former counsel in response to the allegations made against him as well as the respondent’s testimony suggest that the respondent herself made the decision not to procure the testimony of her former husband at proceedings below and then regretted that decision after the Immigration Judge rendered his decision. We do not find that the respondent’s former counsel provided ineffective assistance, nor do we find that the respondent’s proffer constitutes material new evidence which was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the hearing below. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Therefore, the respondent has not met the regulatory requirements for reopening.
 
Finally, it appears that Mr. Ly’s testimony would not have bolstered the respondent’s claims regarding the bona fides of her former marriage but would only have further undermined them. Mr. Ly’s statement that he and the respondent began arguing “¿iºn the spring season of 2000” is inconsistent with the respondent’s January 2006 testimony that she last talked with Mr. Ly in December 1999. Additionally, Mr. Ly’s statement that the respondent’s sister began telling her that he was messing around with the sister’s female employee, whom he had been talking to since early 2000, is inconsistent with the respondent’s testimony that her brother and sister had told her of the woman in June 1999 and that Mr. Ly had met her prior to the respondent’s February 1999 arrival in the United States (Tr. at 54-55).
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 1734676 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE LUIS CORTEZ A.K.A. JOSE ANAYA CASTRO A.K.A. JOSE CORTEZ SOLIS
FILE: AXX XX1 525 - LANCASTER, CA
March 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Brenda C. Diaz, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Catherine Halliday-Roberts
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Continuance
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent’s timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 2, 2008, finding the respondent removable, as charged, based on the respondent’s admissions and concessions of removability (Tr. at 14) as to his 2006 California receiving of stolen property conviction, which he found to be an aggravated felony as defined under section 101(a)(43)(G)of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G); finding he had not demonstrated his eligibility for any for relief from removal (Tr. at 15), and ordering his removal to Mexico, will be dismissed.
 
The only issue raised on appeal is the respondent’s contention that his former attorney rendered ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to request a continuance from the Immigration Court while concomitantly pursuing a post-conviction motion to set aside and vacate the respondent’s 2006 California receiving of stolen property conviction, which underpins the charges of removability, and his eligibility for relief from removal.
 
We find no merit to the contentions raised by the respondent on appeal. To the extent the respondent alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel, we note that in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), we laid out the requirements that a respondent must meet in order to support a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Here, the respondent has failed to comply with any of the established requirements to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Moreover, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that he suffered prejudice as a result of the alleged ineffective assistance of former counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, supra. The record reflects the respondent’s former attorney, after considering the respondent’s entire criminal record which included two probation violations with additional time beyond the original 365 days of his sentence, indicated to the Immigration Judge that, in her professional judgement, it would not be fruitful to pursue any post-conviction relief in this case (Tr. at 15). The respondent has presented no arguments on appeal that would challenge that assessment. We recognize that the present case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, which has held that rigid adherence to the procedural requirements established in Lozada is not always necessary. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir 2000). However, where the respondent has failed to comply with any of the Lozada requirements and where the respondent has also failed to show any prejudice, we are compelled to deny his ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Matter of Assaad, supra.
 
*2 Moreover, the decision to grant or deny a continuance is in the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge, if good cause is shown. See Matter of Luviano-Rodriguez, 21 I&N Dec. 235, 237 (BIA 1996); Matter of Sibrun, 18 I&N Dec. 354 (BIA 1983). That decision will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing. See Matter of Luviano-Rodriguez, supra at 237; Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec.433 (BIA 1987); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.6. There is no indication in the record that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing. An Immigration Judge presented with a facially valid charge of removability need not hold removal proceedings in abeyance based on the mere possibility that a collateral criminal proceeding could have a future impact on the alien’s immigration status. See Contreras v. Schiltgen, 122 F.3d 30, 33-34 (9th Cir. 1997), affd on add’l grounds in Contreras v. Schiltgen, 151 F.3d 906 (9th Cir.1998) (Contreras II) (the court found that the “validity of ¿the alien’sº California conviction can only be tested in an action against the state, which has the greatest interest in preserving its judgment and the best ability to either correct or defend it … and … ¿uºntil ¿the alienº has successfully overturned his conviction in such a proceeding, the ¿Immigration Judgeº is entitled to rely on the conviction as a basis for custody and eventual deportation.”).
 
The respondent raises no arguments on appeal which were not addressed by the Immigration Judge or which would cause us to reverse the Immigration Judge’s decision. Based on the record before us, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is subject to removal from the United States, as charged, based on the respondent’s concessions of removability (Tr. at 14). See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c) (2007). Moreover, the respondent has failed to establish his eligibility for any relief from removal. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 1734676 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 1734675 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA SACARIAS DE PINEDO
FILE: AXX XX6 892 - SAN DIEGO, CA
March 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonio Salazar, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Olga El-Bogdadi
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§ 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated December 20, 2007, which dismissed her appeal from the Immigration Judge’s denial of cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act as a lawful permanent resident, and denied a motion to remand based on allegations of ineffective assistance of former counsel. In so doing, we found that the respondent not only failed to meet any of the requirements for properly asserting an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, but also did not show that she had been actually prejudiced by ineffective assistance. The motion to reopen is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security.
 
The respondent claims that reopening is warranted as she received ineffective assistance of former counsel before the Immigration Judge. A party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue relief previously considered bears a “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings before the Immigration Judge were reopened, with all the attendant delays, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 473 (BIA 1992). The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has stated that an inquiry into whether counsel provided ineffective assistance must begin by determining whether competent counsel would have acted otherwise before examining whether an alien was prejudiced by the alleged ineffective actions of counsel. See Maravilla-Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855, 858 (9 th Cir. 2004). Counsel’s actions must have rendered the proceedings fundamentally unfair such that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting her case. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, 383 F.3d 968, 973 (9th Cir. 2004); Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003). In addition, “substantial prejudice” must be established and such prejudice means that an alien must show that the alleged ineffectiveness affected the outcome of proceedings. See Lara-Torres v. Ashcroft, supra; Lata v. INS, 204 F.3d 1241,1246 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
In support of reopening, the respondent submits a sworn personal declaration and indicates that a copy of the motion and declaration were being sent to her former attorney to allow him an opportunity to respond. However, the respondent has not established that she notified the former counsel of the allegations made against him and gave him an opportunity to respond. Also, the respondent has not indicated whether she filed a bar complaint against former counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Compliance with the Lozada requirements is not optional where there is not an obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel on the record. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004). The respondent continues to assert that had her case been properly prepared and presented the Immigration Judge would have granted the relief she sought. However, the respondent, who asserts that her United States citizen children should have been called as witnesses, has not shown what testimony they would have presented or that it would have affected the outcome of the proceedings. Similarly, she has not shown how additional preparation would have altered the adverse credibility finding below. Consequently, we find that the respondent has not established that any alleged ineffective assistance she may have received caused substantial prejudice. Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
*2 Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 1734675 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2008 WL 1734668 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FELVIS EDITH PORTILLO-GARCIA
FILE: AXX XX3 238 - HOUSTON, TX
March 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Leonel Torres, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Angela A. Crider
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 This case was last before the Board on January 27, 2005, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen. We found that the respondent received proper notice of her hearing and she failed to establish exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear. On May 18, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen before the Board. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.
 
In her motion, the respondent requests that the Board consider the additional facts and evidence supporting the respondent’s claim that she did not receive proper notice of the hearing. The respondent contends that there was improper notice because the address listed on the Notification Requirement for Change of Address lists the wrong address for the respondent, and the transcript of the Immigration Judge’s decision also indicates an incorrect address for the respondent.
 
The respondent is correct that the Notification Requirement for Change of Address (Form I-830) lists an incorrect apartment number for the respondent, and the transcript of the January 29, 2001, proceedings reflects a typographical error regarding the respondent’s apartment number.¿FN1º Tr. at 2. However, both of these errors are harmless and irrelevant, as the notice of hearing dated November 15, 2000, was sent to the respondent at the most recent address she provided. The record reflects that on November 1, 2000, the respondent filed a motion to change venue, indicating that she resided at 7500 Plum Creek Apt. #4237, Houston, TX 77012. On November 14, 2000, the Immigration Judge granted the respondent’s motion to change venue. The order was sent to the respondent at the Plum Creek address, but it was returned to the Houston Immigration Court as undeliverable. Furthermore, on November 15, 2000, the Houston Immigration Court sent a notice of hearing to the respondent at the following address: C/O Elvis Tsikis, 7500 Plum Creek Apt. #4237, Houston, TX 77012. The envelope was returned to the Immigration Court with the handwritten notation “MLNA.” The respondent failed to appear for the hearing on January 29, 2001, and was ordered removed in absentia. The in absentia order also was returned to the Houston Immigration Court with a label indicating “No Forward Order On File - Unable to Forward - Return to Sender.” The Immigration Judge indicated that the notation “MLNA” is customary to reflect “moved, left no address.” See I.J. Dec, dated October 17, 2003, at 2.
 
In her prior motion before the Immigration Court, the respondent claimed that in February 2001, she moved to a new address: 5825 Delaney Rd., #603, Hitchcock, Texas 77563. However, the record contains a Biographic Information Form (Form G-325), dated September 27, 2002, indicating that she began residing at that address in October 2000. Furthermore, the Immigration Judge’s order granting the change of venue motion, the hearing notice dated November 15, 2000, and the in absentia order dated January 29, 2001, were all returned to the Houston Immigration Court as undeliverable, and all of the returned envelopes indicated that the respondent had moved and left no forwarding address. This raises the question whether the respondent moved prior to November 2000, but failed to inform the Immigration Court immediately of her change of address, as required. The respondent received proper notice of her hearing, as it was mailed to the most recent address provided by the respondent and she failed to keep the Immigration Court apprised of her correct address.
 
*2 The respondent now claims for the first time that she failed to appear for her hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel. She asserts that her prior counsel failed to notify her regarding the transfer of venue and the hearing on January 29, 2001. The respondent states that her prior counsel only provided her with a hearing notice dated September 20, 2000, for a hearing on March 30, 2001. We have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the alien has satisfied the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has not complied with any of the requirements set forth in Lozada. Accordingly, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

1

The Department of Homeland Security notes that the respondent herself provided the address on the Form I-830 and signed it.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GLORIA PATRICIA ANDINO
FILE: AXX XX0 200 - SAN JUAN, PR
March 27, 2008
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Joyce A. Phipps, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jorge A. Ramos
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration and reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, from Ecuador, moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider and reopen¿FN1º our decision. On May 26, 1994, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent deported in absentia when she failed to appear at a hearing. On March 8, 2005, the Immigration Judge denied a motion to reopen. On April 14, 2006, we dismissed the appeal of the denied motion to reopen. On November 2, 2007, we denied a motion to reopen which raised the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel and requested equitable tolling for the time limits on motions to reopen. The instant motion will be denied.
 
In our April 14, 2006, decision, we specifically agreed with the Immigration Judge that the respondent had not established that she failed to appear at the hearing before the Immigration Judge due to lack of notice or other exceptional circumstances. In our November 2, 2007, decision, we concluded that the respondent’s argument that the time limits on motions to reopen should be equitably tolled must fail because, during the nearly 10 year period between the Immigration Judge decision and the filing of the first motion, the respondent had not acted with due diligence. See Boakai v. Gonzales, 447 F.3d 1, 3 n.3 (1st Cir. 2006); Jobe v. INS, 238 F.3d 96, 100 (1st Cir. 2001); Cai Xing Chen v. Gonzales, 415 F.3d 151, 154, n 3 (1st Cir. 2005).
 
The instant motion now claims that the respondent was told by 3 attorneys to wait instead of pursuing her case. However, the previous motion filed with the Board complained only about Attorney Manzo, who first represented the respondent while these proceedings were pending. There was no specific complaint alleged or substantiated with regard to the Attorney Napolitano, who filed the motion before the Immigration Judge and represented the respondent on appeal, or with regard to any other counsel.
 
There is no adequate explanation in the instant motion as to why allegations of ineffectiveness were not made against all of the prior attorneys, rather than just the first attorney, in the earlier motion. No evidence of compliance with the procedural or substantive requirements to establish ineffective assistance of counsel claims against any of these other attorneys has been provided. See Lawrence v. Gonzales, 446 F.3d 221, 226 (1st Cir. 2006); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 A motion to reconsider must include the following: (1) an allegation of material factual or legal errors in the prior decision that is supported by pertinent authority; (2) in the case of an affirmance without opinion (“AWO”), a showing that the alleged errors and legal arguments were previously raised on appeal and a statement explaining how the Board erred in affirming the Immigration Judge’s decision under the AWO regulations; and (3) if there has been a change in law, a reference to the relevant statute, regulation, or precedent and an explanation of how the outcome of the Board’s decision is materially affected by the change. Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
Insofar as the motion requests reconsideration of our April 14, 2006, decision on appeal, the motion was not filed until November 30, 2007. The motion is untimely and does not establish an exception. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Insofar as the motion requests reconsideration of our November 2, 2007, decision, the argument in the motion merely disagrees with our conclusion, but does not establish errors of fact or law supported by pertinent authority, or cite to changed law, that would warrant granting the motion. See Matter of O-S-G-, supra. Insofar as the motion attempts to argue new facts, these are not substantiated by any objective evidence. Furthermore, the respondent does not explain why these facts were not introduced in the earlier motion to reopen denied by the Board. 8 C.F.R § 1003.2(c). Accordingly, the motion is denied. The request for a stay is also denied.
 
FOR THE BOARD
Frederick D. Hess

1

The motion is styled a motion to reconsider, but as it includes purportedly new evidence it will also be treated as a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2.

IN RE: AHMED TAHA
FILE: AXX XX5 199 - CHICAGO, IL
March 24, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Raymond J. Sanders, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Micheal L. Harper
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration and reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on November 7, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the Immigration Judge’s June 8, 2006, decision denying a continuance of proceedings. The respondent now moves to reconsider and reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed an opposition to the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider must be filed within 30 days of the Board’s decision and assert that the Board made a factual or legal error at the time it rendered the previous decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b) (2003); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991), aff’d, 979 F.2d 212 (11th Cir. 1992) (table case). The respondent acknowledges that his motion is untimely but notes that his former attorney did not notify him of the Board’s decision in time to seek reconsideration. Even if timely, the respondent failed to allege any legal or factual error by the Board in our prior order to support reconsideration. Thus, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
The respondent also seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue an application for adjustment of status pursuant to section 245(i)ofthe Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255(i). With respect to the new evidence submitted in conjunction with the instant motion to reopen, we find that this evidence could have been presented at the hearing before the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) (providing that a motion to reopen shall be supported by new evidence that is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented previously). The respondent argues in his motion, however, that his prior counsel “failed to investigate and submit evidence either to the IJ or BIA that Respondent is grandfathered under U.S.C. § 245(i) of the LIFE Act.” If the respondent is alleging a claim of ineffective assistance by his former counsel, he has failed to comply with any of the requirements for asserting such a claim outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637(BIA 1988).¿FN1º See Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498, 501 (7th Cir. 2001) (recognizes Board’s authority to rule and analyze cases within the Lozada framework). Moreover, regardless of the respondent’s claim of derivative status as a grandfathered alien under section 245(i) of the Act, as we noted in our prior decision, the respondent failed to present evidence that his I-130 petition has been approved or that an immigrant visa is immediately available to him. Thus, the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD

Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GLORIA HERNANDEZ DE DIAZ
FILE: AXX XX0 666 - SAN DIEGO, CA
March 24, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Christopher Duran, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jesus Clemente
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a decision dated July 18, 2007, the Board of Immigration Appeals denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen and reconsider. The respondent’s motion will be granted and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen and reconsider is both untimely and number barred. The respondent requests equitable tolling based on the ineffective assistance of former counsel (Patricia Vargas). The respondent shows substantial compliance with the requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), to allege ineffective assistance of former counsel. The respondent presents a detailed affidavit and a copy of the attorney complaint form filed with the California State Bar. Although the respondent did not write a letter directly to Ms. Vargas, the certificate of service for the respondent’s motion states that a copy of the motion and attachments was sent to Ms. Vargas, thus giving her an opportunity to respond to the allegations against her.
 
The respondent shows sufficient prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (holding that the alien did not establish that his former counsel’s failure to file a timely appeal constituted sufficient prejudice to warrant consideration of his late appeal on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel). The respondent offers documents (which Ms. Vargas did not present in support of the two motions she filed for the respondent) which indicate the respondent’s husband filed for legalization, her daughter filed for benefits under the Family Unity Program (FUP), and her husband received two receipts for fees paid for two FUP applications. These documents do not prove that the respondent was granted FUP status. The respondent and her husband, however, submit their respective declarations. The respondent states that she applied for derivative FUP status through her husband in 1991. The respondent’s husband states that his FUP application for his wife was granted. In Celis-Castellano v. Ashcroft, 298 F.3d 888, 892 (9th Cir. 2002), the court stated that facts presented in affidavits supporting a motion to reopen must be accepted as true unless inherently unbelievable. Under the circumstances present here, it is not “inherently unbelievable” that the respondent was granted FUP status. Depending on if and when FUP status was granted, when combined with the respondent’s admission as a lawful permanent resident in May of 2000, she may meet the 7 year continuous residence (after having been admitted in any status) requirement in section 240A(a)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a)(2), and thus be eligible for cancellation of removal. See Garcia-Quintero v. Gonzales, 455 F.3d 1006 (9th Cir. 2006) (holding that acceptance into FUP constitutes being “admitted in any status” for the purposes of section 240A(a)(2) of the Act).
 
*2 The respondent shows due diligence. See generally Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003) (finding that the limitation period may be tolled until the alien meets with new counsel to discuss his file, thereby becoming aware of the harm resulting from the misconduct of his prior representatives). The second motion by Ms. Vargas was denied on July 18, 2007. The respondent retained present counsel on July 27, 2007, filled out the attorney complaint form on August 15, 2007, executed her declaration on August 20, 2007, and filed the present motion on August 27, 2007. Due diligence has been shown.
 
On remand both parties may submit further evidence regarding whether the respondent was granted FUP status, and if so, when she was granted such status. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
Thomas G. Snow
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 1734650 (BIA)

2008 WL 762668 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: DEXTER LANCELOT RODNEY A.K.A. RODNEY DEXTER A.K.A. DEXTER RODNOY
FILE: AXX XX7 175 - YORK, PA
March 6, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sandra Greene, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
  
APPLICATION: Termination
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s November 6, 2007, decision finding him removable under section 237(a)(2XA)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. He also moves to remand, asserting that his trial attorney rendered ineffective assistance in conceding his removability (Tr. at 20-21). The Department of Homeland Security has not filed an appeal brief or a response to the motion to remand. The motion will be granted, and the case will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
The respondent has complied with the evidentiary requirements for asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988).¿FN1º SeeLu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 132 (3rd Cir. 2001) (approving the Lozada requirements). In order to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance, the Third Circuit has stated that an alien “must demonstrate prejudice, he ‘must show that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.”’ Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369, 377 (3rd Cir. 2002), citing Uspango v. Asheroft, 289 F.3d 226, 231 (3rd Cir. 2002).
 
We conclude that the respondent has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that a competent attorney would have challenged the respondent’s removability based on the aggravated felony charge listed in the Notice to Appear. As the respondent outlines in his appeal brief, there are arguable issues as to whether the DHS met its burden of establishing that the respondent’s bank fraud conviction involved a loss to a victim or victims exceeding $10,000 to constitute an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(M) of the Act. Because the respondent “was prevented from reasonably presenting his case,” we find that he established the requisite prejudice to support a remand for a hearing on removability. See Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, supra.
 
Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s November 6, 2007, decision will be vacated, the motion to remand will be granted, and the case remanded to Immigration Court for further proceedings.
 
ORDER: The Immigration Judge’s November 6, 2007, decision is hereby vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
*2 John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD

Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARINO CABRAL
FILE: AXX XX7 370 - BOSTON, MA
March 3, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Adam S. Elman, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(l)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
In a decision dated November 1, 2006, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has appealed that decision. The appeal is dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen as untimely because it was filed on September 8, 2005, more than 90 days after the respondent was ordered removed on July 2, 2001. See 8C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). Moreover, the Immigration Judge did not find that exceptional circumstances were present, even if such circumstances could justify the untimeliness of the respondent’s motion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). The Immigration Judge found that the respondent failed to demonstrate that he received ineffective assistance of counsel, because he did not satisfy any of the procedural requirements for attorney negligence as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1stCir. 1988), nor has he explained his failure to do so (I.J. at 4, 5). Failure to meet the Lozada requirements will usually be fatal to the claim. See Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996) (discussion of policy considerations underlying Lozada requirements); aff’d Rivera v. INS, 122 F. 3d 1062 (4th Cir. 1997). The respondent refers to the Ninth Circuit case, Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000), and contends that strict adherence to the Lozada requirements is not required when the record of ineffective assistance of counsel is legitimate and substantial. At the outset, we note that this case arises in the jurisdiction of the First Circuit, and the respondent has not shown that the First Circuit has adopted the reasoning of the Ninth Circuit in Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra. The First Circuit has held that the Lozada requirements may be applied as long as the court does not arbitrarily demand adherence to them. Saakian v. INS, 252 F.3d 21, 26 (1st Cir. 2001); I.J. at 4.¿FN1º Here, the failure to satisfy any of the Lozada requirements is fatal to this claim. Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9 (1st Cir. 2004). Because the respondent’s motion to reopen was filed untimely, the appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion is dismissed.
 
*2 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

The Board has held that attorney negligence is not an exception to a statutory time limit for the filing of a motion to reopen. Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) and Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ETZHAK NIMNI
FILE: AXX XX3 973 - EL PASO, TX
February 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Victoria Calle, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Corine Dominguez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated December 6, 2007. Therein, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny his motion to reopen an order of removal entered in absentia on June 29, 1999. The record will be remanded.
 
A motion to reconsider is a “request that the Board reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked.” Matter of Ramos, 23 I&N Dec. 336, 338 (BIA 2002) (quoting Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 n.2 (BIA 1991)). A motion to reconsider challenges the Board’s original decision and alleges that it is defective in some regard. Matter of Cerna, supra, at 402. A motion to reconsider contests the correctness of the original decision based on the previous factual record, as opposed to a motion to reopen, which seeks a new hearing based on new or previously unavailable evidence. Id at 403; see also section 240(c)(7)(B) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) and Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006).
 
The respondent argues in his motion that the Board incorrectly concluded that he was notified of the hearing. He states that his former counsel withdrew as his representative on May 19, 1999, and that he did not receive a notice of hearing either from his former counsel or from the court. The respondent further argues that there is no proof that the Immigration Court actually mailed the Notice of Hearing to him. In addition, the respondent argues in his motion that the Board incorrectly determined that he failed to satisfy the procedural requirements for showing ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). He contends that former counsel was notified of the complaint filed against him with the Texas Bar Association as a part of the bar complaint process.
 
We find in light of the allegation of non-receipt of the hearing notice that it is appropriate to remand these proceedings to the Immigration Judge to more fully address whether the respondent was adequately notified of the hearing. See Maknojiya v. Gonzales, 432 F.3d 588, 589 (5th Cir. 2005) (noting the lower burden required for rebutting the presumption of delivery for correspondence sent by regular mail was met where both alien and his counsel submitted affidavits indicating that they did not receive the notice that reset the hearing from an earlier hearing date than originally scheduled). In addition, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge did not address the issue of whether the respondent was victimized by the negligence of his former counsel, Mr. Rosenbloom. We find it appropriate for the Immigration Judge to render the initial findings of fact on the issue.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reconsider is granted, and these proceedings are remanded for further proceedings.
 
John Guendelsberger
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 762745 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.






2008 WL 762742 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EDWIN FRU NGUTI
FILE: AXX XX6 806 - BUFFALO, NY
February 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William H. Berger, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Daniel M. Griebel
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º -In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Cameroon, appeals an Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 21, 2006. On August 4, 2006, the respondent failed to appear for a hearing pursuant to a notice mailed to his address on June 22, 2006, and the Immigration Judge ordered him removed to Cameroon in absentia (I.J. at 2). On September 1, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to rescind the removal order and reopen proceedings with the Immigration Judge alleging that he failed to appear for the Immigration Court hearing due to a lack of notice. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C). Following the Immigration Judge’s September 21, 2006, denial of the respondent’s motion, the respondent filed a timely appeal with the Board. The request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7). The respondent’s appeal will be sustained and the removal proceedings will be reopened.
 
As an initial matter, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion upon concluding that the respondent failed to show — either through an affidavit or other evidence — that he did not receive notice of the August 4, 2006, hearing (I.J. at 2-3). Although the Immigration Judge concluded that the respondent did not rebut the presumption of effective delivery, we find that the removal order should be rescinded. First, we note that the respondent initiated correspondence with the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) as a result of his prior submission of his asylum application, which was confirmed by the DHS arresting officer in his notation in the Record of Deportable/Inadmissible Alien. As a result, the respondent arguably had little motive to avoid the scheduled hearing (Respondent’s Br. at 1, 6). See generally Lopes v. Gonzales, 468 F.3d 81, 86 (2d Cir. 2006) (remanding, in part, because the Board did not consider all relevant evidence offered to rebut the presumption of delivery, including the fact that the petitioner — through the filing of an application for a labor certification — initiated a proceeding to obtain a benefit, which makes it less likely that he would ignore, or have such motive to, an immigration proceeding for which he had notice); see also Alrefae v. Chertoff, 471 F.3d 353, 359 (2d Cir. 2006). Also, the respondent did not delay in seeking a reopening of the proceedings as he, upon receiving the in absentia removal order, apparently retained Mr. Mark Kenmore as his counsel, who filed the motion on September 1, 2006.
 
*2 In addition, while not discussed in the Immigration Judge’s decision, the DHS in this matter did not oppose the respondent’s motion to rescind the removal order and reopen proceedings (Respondent’s Br. at 2). Indeed, as evidenced through the DHS’s statement on appeal, it continues to assert its non-opposition to the respondent’s motion (Respondent’s Br. at 3, 6). Moreover, as indicated in the respondent’s brief, the respondent’s prior attorney, Mr. Kenmore, may have rendered ineffective assistance to the respondent prior to his withdrawal of the case (Respondent’s Br. at 3-4). Although the respondent has not satisfied the procedural requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to demonstrate ineffective assistance of counsel, he suggests that such defective representation is apparent from the record as Mr. Kenmore — despite suffering from cognitive difficulties and memory problems and being under the care of a neurologist since June 2006 — continued to represent the respondent before the Immigration Judge and the Board. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Also, the respondent offers the fact that no supporting documentation was provided with the motion to the Immigration Judge as further evidence of such inadequate representation.¿FN1º
 
Therefore, we will sustain the respondent’s appeal, vacate the Immigration Judge’s decision, and rescind the in absentia order. As we find that the proceedings should be reopened, the record will be remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings as may be appropriate under the circumstances. See Matter of Patel, 16 I&N Dec. 600 (BIA 1978). Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is sustained.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is hereby remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this order.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

Although the respondent now presents sworn affidavits from himself and his uncle attesting to the non-delivery of the June 22, 2006, notice (Respondent’s Br. at 5-6), we note that the Board may not accept such documents into the record as it is precluded from engaging in factfinding in the course of deciding appeals. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(iv).

2008 WL 762758 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EVERTON ALPHONSO MCKENZIE
FILE: AXX XX4 706 - MIAMI, FL
February 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Geoffrey A. Hoffman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David J. Kelly
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
  
APPLICATION: Section 212(c) waiver
 
This case was last before us on May 28, 2003, when we remanded the record of proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s application for a waiver of inadmissibility under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c), in light of INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001). On February 13, 2007, the Immigration Judge pretermitted the respondent’s application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s February 13, 2007, decision. The respondent has also filed a motion to remand in order to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) filed an opposition to the respondent’s motion to remand. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed and his motion to remand will be denied. Additionally, the respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(e)(7).
 
On appeal, the respondent does not contest that he was convicted by a jury for the offense of criminal possession of marijuana in the fourth degree and criminally using drug paraphernalia in the second degree. Instead, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge was bound by the law of our May 28, 2003, remand to consider his application for section 212(c) relief. The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge erred by holding that he is ineligible for section 212(c) relief because he was convicted pursuant to a jury trial, rather than pursuant to a guilty plea. Additionally, the respondent raises due process arguments. In his motion to remand, the respondent contends that his proceedings should be remanded to the Immigration Judge so that he can apply for relief under section 212(h) of the Act.
 
Turning first to the respondent’s application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent is ineligible for section 212(c) relief (I. J. at 2). Specifically, we find that the respondent’s conviction by jury trial does not fall within the ambit of the United States Supreme Court’s decision in INS v. St. Cyr, 533 U.S. 289 (2001) (holding that the repeal of section 212(c) of the Act by the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act (“IIRIRA”) could not be applied retroactively to aliens who pled guilty or nolo contendere to a crime prior to IIRIRA’s enactment). See Alexandre v. United States Attorney General 452 F.3d 1204, 1207 (11th Cir. 2006) (finding that a section 212(c) waiver was precluded because the alien had proceeded to trial and was convicted by jury). To the extent that the respondent relies on the decisions in other federal courts of appeal that have found that section 212(c) relief may be available to aliens who were convicted after a jury trial, we find that those decisions are not binding on the present matter as they did not arise in the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit (the “Eleventh Circuit’), the jurisdiction in which this case arises. See Matter of Anselmo, 20 I&N Dec. 25, 31 (BIA 1989) (explaining that the Board historically follows a court’s precedent in cases arising in that circuit). Moreover, we reject the respondent’s contention on appeal that the Eleventh Circuit has only issued decisions containing non-binding dicta indicating that relief under former section 212(c) of the Act is unavailable to aliens who have been convicted pursuant to a jury trial. See Alexandre v. United States Attorney General, supra, at 1207. We also reject the respondent’s assertion on appeal that the law of the case doctrine forbad the Immigration Judge from pretermitting his application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act. Although we remanded the record of proceedings to the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s application for relief under former section 212(c) of the Act in light of INS v. St. Cyr, supra, the respondent concedes on appeal that our remand was based on a conviction document that erroneously indicated that he had been convicted pursuant to a guilty plea. See respondent’s motion to remand at pg. 3, n.1. In this regard, we reject the respondent’s argument on appeal that it was incumbent upon the DHS to determine prior to our remand that the respondent had been convicted pursuant to a jury trial rather than pursuant to a guilty plea. Furthermore, to the extent that the respondent argues that the relevant law as applied to him is unconstitutional, we have no jurisdiction over the constitutionality of the statutes and regulations we administer. See Matter of C-, 20 I&N Dec. 529, 532 (BIA 1992).
 
*2 Turning next to the respondent’s motion to remand, we find that remand of proceedings is not warranted. With his motion to remand, the respondent has submitted an application for relief under section 212(h) of the Act, as well as supporting documentation. We find that the respondent has not adequately demonstrated that the proffered evidence with his motion to remand was not previously available or could not have been presented during his hearing before the Immigration Judge. See 8 C.F.R. § 1002.3(c)(1), (c)(4); see generally Matter of R-R-, 20 I&N Dec. 547 (BIA 1992) (providing that an alien seeking reopening based on a failure to request relief before the completion of deportation proceedings must meet the general motion requirements). Moreover, we note that, even if the Immigration Judge failed to advise the respondent of his right to apply for relief under section 212(h) of the Act during the initial proceedings, as argued by the respondent in his motion, the respondent was nonetheless aware of the possibility for such relief during the remanded proceedings and still failed to apply at that time (Tr. at 16-17). To the extent that the respondent claims that his failure to request relief under section 212(h) of the Act before the Immigration Judge was the result of ineffective assistance by his counsel, he has made no attempt to comply with the procedural requirements set forth under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing a valid claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the following order will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 762758 (BIA)
End of Document

IN RE: XUAN QUAN
FILE: AXX XX3 565 - SAN ANTONIO, TX
February 27, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Albert Chow, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s May 10, 2007, decision denying her¿FN1º motion to rescind the order of removal entered in her case on an in absentia basis. We have considered the respondent’s appellate arguments as set forth on her Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26), her statement attached thereto, and in her subsequently-filed brief. Inasmuch as we agree with the Immigration Judge’s resolution of this case, we adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision, with the exception of the discussion found on pages 5 and 6 suggesting frivolous behavior on the part of the respondent’s current attorney. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994).
 
On appeal, the respondent’s current attorney asserts that the respondent’s supporting declaration sufficiently establishes that she did not receive notice of her March 5, 2007, hearing from her former attorney. See Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26); Respondent’s Brief on Appeal. We disagree. In that declaration, the respondent indicates only that upon her release from detention she provided her new address in New Jersey to the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and to the Immigration Court. She makes no specific claims of having provided this address to her former attorney, Ronald Higgins,¿FN2º having kept in touch with attorney Higgins, or that attorney Higgins failed to apprise her that a hearing would be conducted in her case on March 5, 2007. See Exh. 8 (Motion, attached declaration).
 
There also is no indication that prior to the March 5th proceeding attorney Higgins was granted a withdrawal of his representation of the respondent, or that substitute counsel entered an appearance in his stead. Rather, the record reflects that attorney Higgins appeared at the hearing as scheduled, and that the Immigration Judge found he offered no reasonable cause accounting for the respondent’s absence. See Exh. 6 (March 5, 2007, I.J. dec); see also May 10, 2007, I.J. dec at 5.
 
Under the circumstances presented, the respondent was given proper notice of her March 5, 2007, hearing in accordance with the federal regulatory requirements. See I.J. at 5; 8 C.F.R. § 1292.5(a) (2007); see also Men Keng Chang v. Jiugni, 669 F.2d 275, 277(5th Cir. 1982); Matter of Barocio, 19 I&N Dec. 255 (BIA 1985); Exh. 3 (Form EOIR-28, filed November 17, 2006, in Immigration Court by attorney Higgins) and Exh. 5 (hearing notice). In arguing otherwise, the respondent’s reliance on case law handed down by the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit is misplaced, inasmuch as the instant case arises in the Fifth, not the Ninth, Circuit.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent’s current attorney asserts that the respondent received ineffective assistance by her former attorney. However, none of the requirements for advancing such a claim, as discussed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), have been satisfied. Citing Ninth Circuit case law, the respondent contends that compliance with the Lozada requirements are not necessary in this case. See Brief, pp. 4-5. However, as has already been noted Ninth Circuit case law is not controlling here. In any event, the Fifth Circuit has specifically held that to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien in removal proceedings must fulfillthe Lozada requirements and must demonstrate that counsel’s actions were prejudicial to his case. See Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162, 165 (5th Cir. 2006); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir. 2000). No such showings have been made here.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Neil P. Miller
FOR THE BOARD

2008 WL 655897 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RODRIGO OCTAVIO GONZALEZ
FILE: AXX XX8 082 - LOS ANGELES, CA
February 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Steven E. Comer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kristin Piepmeier
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on November 14, 2006, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision denying his motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order entered on January 17, 2006.
 
On December 26, 2007, the respondent, a native and citizen of El Salvador, filed a motion to reopen and rescind the January 17, 2006, in absentia removal order. The respondent’s motion is based, in part, on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel by his two prior attorneys, Mr. David Paz Soldan and Mr. James F. Kajtoch.
 
We will reopen these proceedings on our own motion because we failed to consider the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims against his prior attorneys, Mr. Paz Soldan and Mr. Kajtoch, which he raised on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent’s appeal brief, filed on September 25, 2006, detailed the allegations of his prior attorneys’ wrongdoing. When the respondent presented these claims on appeal, he proffered documentation that established his compliance with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 4-9, Exhibit 4. Yet, we did not consider these claims. See Board’s November 14, 2006, decision at 5.
 
The ineffective assistance of counsel claims raised by the respondent on appeal are the same as those he presents in the instant motion. He claims that on January 17, 2006, he arrived at 8:15 a.m. for his scheduled 8:30 A.M. removal hearing. When he did not find his attorney, Mr. Paz Soldan, he called the law office and was instructed to wait outside the courtroom until his attorney arrived. Mr. Paz Soldan did not arrive until 9:30 A.M. By the time they entered the courtroom together it was empty. When Mr. Paz Soldan failed to file a promised motion to reopen, the respondent retained Mr. Kajtoch.
 
According to the respondent’s August 31, 2006, statement, which was submitted on appeal, when Mr. Kajtoch assisted him in filing a motion to reopen he insisted that the respondent state that he understood that the hearing was scheduled for 9:30 A.M. and that he arrived at the courtroom at 9:15 A.M. The respondent claims that he did not want to tell a lie, but Mr. Kajtoch insisted that it was necessary to obtain reopening. Mr. Kajtoch also advised the respondent not to file a Lozada claim against Mr. Paz Soldan. The respondent also asserted that Mr. Kajtoch erred by failing to support the respondent’s motions to reopen with any applications for relief from removal. See Respondent’s Appeal Brief at 5-9, Attachment 4 at 2-3. Notably, there is no evidence to establish that Mr. Paz Soldan or Mr. Kajtoch ever responded to the respondent’s allegations.
 
*2 Had we adjudicated the respondent’s ineffective assistance of counsel claims which he raised on appeal, we would have remanded this case for further proceedings. The respondent followed the directives of both his prior attorneys, Mr. Paz Soldan and Mr. Kajtoch. Had the respondent not relied on his attorneys’ bad advice, he would have appeared for his hearing or obtained reopening. See Monjaraz-Munoz, 327 F.3d 892 (9th Cir. 2003)(if an alien fails to appear because of his actual and reasonable reliance on counsel’s erroneous advice, it can constitute an exceptional circumstance beyond the control of the alien). Based on the foregoing, we find it appropriate to reopen these proceedings on our own motion. Because we find it appropriate to reopen these proceedings, we will not address the remaining issues raised by the respondent in this instant motion.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

IN RE: JOSE SOSA

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sigang Li, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Certification
 
*1 On September 19, 2007, the respondent filed this appeal from an Immigration Judge’s July 2, 2007, decision. It is undisputed that the appeal, which was due on or before August 1, 2007, is untimely. With the Notice of Appeal, the respondent has submitted a motion in which he asserts that his former attorney’s mishandling of his case resulted in the untimeliness of the appeal. We will construe the respondent’s motion as a motion for certification to the Board. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (c). The motion will be denied and the appeal will be dismissed.
 
In his motion, the respondent merely asserts that “(h)is counsel reserved the appeal …(h)owever, such appeal was never filed.” He has not, however, provided evidence that he entered into an agreement with his former attorney to filed an appeal. See generally Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (specifying the procedural requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim in the context of motions filed before the Board). Although he has submitted a copy of a complaint against his former attorney to the Disciplinary Board of the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania, the respondent has not submitted proof that the complaint has been properly filed, and he has not submitted a statement of the alleged misrepresentations. The evidence submitted with the motion simply fails to show that ineffective assistance of counsel caused the untimeliness of the appeal. Accordingly, the motion for certification will be denied, and the untimely appeal will be dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a), 1003.38, and 1240.15. The Immigration Judge’s decision is now final, and the record will be returned to the Immigration Court.¿FN1º See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.39 and 1240.14.
 
The following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion for certification to the Board is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The untimely appeal is dismissed for lack of jurisdiction.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The respondent’s appeal has been dismissed for lack of jurisdiction. Either party wishing to file a motion in this case should follow the following guidelines: a motion to reconsider challenging the finding that the appeal was untimely must be filed with the Board. However, a motion challenging any other finding or seeking reopening of the proceedings must be filed with the Immigration Court. See Matter of Mladineo, 14 I&N Dec. 591 (BIA 1974); Matter of Lopez, 22 I&N Dec. 16 (BIA 1998). Strict time and number limits apply to motions to reconsider and motions to reopen before the Immigration Judge and this Board. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(2) and 1003.23(b)(1).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ALI ABDO MOHSEN MOHAMED QAYED ALNAHHAM
FILE: AXX XX1 368 - PHOENIX, AZ
February 6, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ronald G. Finch, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
John R. Holya
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º -In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, appeals from the Immigration Judge’s March 22, 2007, decision denying her “Motion to Reconsider.” The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The procedural history of this case is as follows. The respondent was ordered removed from the United States on June 29, 2004, because she failed to appear for a hearing. On March 31, 2006, she filed a motion to reopen alleging that she did not receive notice of the hearing, which was granted by the Immigration Judge on April 17, 2006. The respondent was given until January 24, 2007, to file an application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). When the respondent failed to timely file her application for cancellation of removal, the Immigration Judge found that her application had been abandoned and granted her voluntary departure in a decision dated February 7, 2007. On February 26, 2007, the respondent filed a motion to reopen, which the Immigration Judge denied on March 6, 2007. The respondent then filed a “Motion to Reconsider,” which the Immigration Judge denied on March 22, 2007. The respondent now appeals from the Immigration Judge’s denial of her “Motion to Reconsider.” On appeal, the respondent seeks reopening of proceedings because she was the victim of ineffective assistance. Specifically, the respondent contends that she failed to timely file her application for cancellation of removal because her current counsel failed to inform her in writing of her filing deadline.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s “Motion to Reconsider.” To the extent that the respondent’s “Motion to Reconsider”challenged the merits of the Immigration Judge’s decision denying her motion to reopen, we find that she failed to establish any material error of fact or law in the Immigration Judge’s decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); see also Matter of O-S-G-, 24 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2006). Specifically, we find that the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent failed to file a copy of her application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act with her motion to reopen, as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). Because we find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen for the above mentioned reason, we need not address the Immigration Judge’s additional finding that the respondent’s motion to reopen was number-barred. To the extent that the respondent sought to submit a copy of her application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act with her “Motion to Reconsider,” we construe her motion as a motion to reopen rather than as a motion to reconsider. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (providing that a motion to reopen is granted upon a proffer of new, material evidence); cf. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (providing that a motion to reconsider challenges determinations of law and fact). Since this was the respondent’s second motion to reopen filed after her in absentia removal order had been reopened, we find that it was number-barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
*2 Moreover, to the extent that the respondent seeks to submit new evidence on appeal in the form of a self-reporting letter to the Arizona State Bar from her current counsel alleging that he engaged in ineffective assistance, we are precluded from engaging in fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(iv) (providing that, subject to certain exceptions not applicable to the present case, the Board will not engage in fact-finding in the course of deciding appeals and that a party asserting that the Board cannot properly resolve an appeal without further fact-finding must file a motion to remand). Furthermore, we find that remand of proceedings based on this new evidence is not warranted. The letter does not concede that he and his assistants failed orally to provide the respondent with notice of the application deadline but merely contends that he should have done so in writing. Moreover, the respondent has not met the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for establishing a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 60 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to voluntarily depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the DHS, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $ 1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
 
Roger A Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
