2008 WL 486927 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YOVANNA DE LOS ANGELES NUNEZ A.K.A. SANDRA SANTIAGO
FILE: AXX XX5 211 - BOSTON, MA
January 31, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lidia Sanchez, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Rescission
 
*1 ORDER: PER CURIAM. The respondent was ordered removed in absentia in a December 4, 2006, Immigration Judge’s decision. She filed a motion to reopen proceedings on January 3, 2007, alleging that former counsel provided her with ineffective assistance as he misrepresented his handling of the case, and failed to inform her of her hearing date and time. In a February 14, 2007, decision an Immigration Judge denied the motion, finding the respondent had not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent’s appeal of this decision is dismissed.
 
The Board has held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). Matter of Lozada requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him or her and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639; see also Betouche v. Ashcroft, 357F.3d 147, 149 (1st Cir. 2004) (citing the above-noted requirements).
 
The Immigration Judge correctly found the respondent failed to comply with the requirements enumerated in Matter of Lozada, supra, as she did not show that she had notified former counsel of her claim, and filed a complaint against him with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. We find no merit to the respondent’s vague and generalized allegations that the Immigration Judge’s decision was “arbitrary and capricious,” an “abuse of discretion,” and “against the law” (Notice of Appeal).
 
*2 The respondent has not expounded on these ambiguous and unsupported assertions, and we find no support for the same.
 
On appeal the respondent submitted a letter which she reportedly filed with the bar authorities complaining about former counsel’s actions. However, we normally do not consider new evidence submitted on appeal as the record we review on appeal is the record before the Immigration Judge. Matter of H-A-, 20 I&N Dec. 529 (BIA 1992); Matter of Haim, 19 I&N Dec. 641 (BIA 1988); Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984) (the Board is an appellate body whose function is to review, not to create, a record.). Moreover, we do not find that a remand in this regard is warranted. There is no evidence that this letter was, in fact, filed with the authorities as alleged. Further, there is no evidence that the respondent’s former counsel has been informed of the allegations against him and afforded an opportunity to respond.¿FN1º See Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9, 10-12 (1st Cir. 2004) (finding that counsel targeted by an ineffective assistance claim must “be provided an opportunity to respond before filing the motion to reopen”(emphasis added)). Therefore, as the respondent to date has yet to comply with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, a remand in this regard is not appropriate. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2; see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992).
 
In sum, as the respondent has not established that she failed to appear at her hearing due to her former representative’s ineffective assistance, the Immigration Judge did not err in denying her motion to reopen. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
Footnotes

1

The circuit where this case arises has declined to find that the mere lodging of a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel with the bar authorities functionally satisfies the notice requirement. Betouche v. Ashcroft, 357 F.3d at 151 (holding that the filing “simply established that ¿ the petitionerº had notified the Board of Bar Overseers of his complaint, not that either he or the Board had notified ¿prior counselº.”). Moreover, as noted, there is no objective evidence that such a claim has, in fact, been filed.

2008 WL 486949 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANTONINA SURGANOVA A.K.A. ANTONINA BEAUDOIN
FILE: AXX XX4 919 - CHICAGO, IL
January 31, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James C. Ten Broeck, Jr., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ellen J. Krupp
Assistant Chief Counsel, ICE
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
  
APPLICATION: Termination of proceedings
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s July 17, 2006, decision dismissing the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent, a native of Lithuania who asserts that she is a citizen of the Ukraine, entered the United States as a visitor in April 2000 as a visitor, and adjusted her status to that of immigrant in November 2003, based upon the approval of a Petition for Alien Relative, filed by the respondent’s husband. After interviewing the respondent’s husband about the bona fides of the relationship, the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”) initiated removal proceedings against the respondent, charging her with having engaged in marriage fraud.
 
We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is removable under section 237(a)(1)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act (the “Act”), 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A), because she procured her adjustment of status by marriage fraud under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act. (I.J. at 3-4, 16-17). Section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act states: “Any alien who, by fraud or willfully misrepresenting a material fact, seeks to procure (or has sought to procure or has procured) a visa, other documentation, or entry into the United States or other benefit provided under this Act is excludable.”
 
The central question in determining the good faith of a marriage is whether the bride and groom intended to establish a life together at the time they were married. See, e.g., Matter of Soriano, 19 I&N Dec. 764, 765 (BIA 1988); Matter of Laureano, 19 I&N Dec. 1, 2-3 (BIA 1983). Evidence of such intent may include, but is not limited to, proof that the spouse has been listed on insurance policies, proof of joint property leases, income tax forms, bank accounts, a period of courtship, testimony of mutual experiences and a shared life, and the like. It also may include documentation relating to the degree to which the financial assets and liabilities of the parties were combined; documentation concerning the length of time during which the parties cohabited after the marriage and after the alien obtained permanent residence; and any other evidence supporting the bona fides of the marriage. See Matter of Laureano, supra; Matter of Phillis, 15 I&N Dec. 385 (BIA 1975); see also 8 C.F.R. §§ 1216.4(a)(5) and 1216.5 (e)(2).
 
*2 In the present case, the respondent’s husband (a United States citizen petitioner) signed a statement in August 2004 in which he indicated that his marriage with the respondent was not consummated, and that he just wanted to help her become an American citizen (Exh. 2). He also described how the respondent’s daughter and son-in-law paid him, and helped arrange the marriage (Exh. 2). The petitioner now testifies that he signed under duress, and that his statement should not be considered (Tr. at 53, 73-74). We agree with the Immigration Judge that more weight should be given to the testimony of the special agents who interviewed the petitioner than to the testimony of the petitioner (I.J. at 10, 15), and that the petitioner’s statement should be considered.
 
The respondent has argued that in a number of instances, the testimony of the special agents was irrelevant, speculative, lacked foundation, and called for conclusions of law and/or opinions, and that because it was admitted into evidence, it rendered the proceedings unfair and was in violation of the due process clause of the Constitution (R brief at 6-9). We point out that in administrative removal proceedings, the Federal Rules of Evidence are not controlling. See Matter of Grijalva, 19 I&N Dec. 713 (BIA 1988); see also Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377 (BIA 1986). Rather, under the applicable federal regulations, the Immigration Judge may receive into evidence any oral or written statement which is material and relevant to any issue in the case previously made by the alien or any other person during any investigation or examination. See 8 C.F.R. § 1236.1 (2005); see generally 8 C.F.R. § 1242.14. The testimony of the special agents was certainly probative as to the circumstances surrounding the petitioner’s signing his statement. Likewise, the witness testimony permitted by the Immigration Judge was subject to cross examination by the respondent pursuant to section 240(b)(4)(B), and we thus find the reliance on such testimony by the Immigration Judge to be fundamentally fair. Furthermore, the Immigration Judge did not state that he was assigning more weight to the testimony of the special agents because of the disputed testimony that the respondent referred to.
 
Regarding the disputed testimony, the respondent argues that her first counsel of record should have cross-examined the special agents, and that her failure to do so constituted ineffective assistance of counsel (R brief at 8-9). The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge unreasonably held that the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988); aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) could not be deviated from (Tr. at 231-34). We note that a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must be supported by: 1) an affidavit by the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; 2) a showing that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and 3) a showing that a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, or if not, why not. See id. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not complied with the first requirement of Lozada, i.e., submitting an affidavit detailing the agreement that she had with her first attorney (Tr. at 231-34). Although several circuits have held that aliens only need to show substantial compliance with Lozada, the United States Court of Appeals for the Seventh Circuit (the “Seventh Circuit”), under whose jurisdiction the current case arises, is not one of those circuits. See Patel v. Gonzales, 496 F. 3d 829, 831 (7th Cir. 2007) (Court notes that while several Circuits have held that aliens only need to show substantial compliance with the requirements of Lozada, the Seventh Circuit has not expressly decided whether the Board abuses its discretion by requiring strict compliance with Lozada).
 
*3 Furthermore, even if is conceded that the requirements of Lozada were substantially complied with, the actions of counsel that are in question, i.e., allowing the aforementioned testimony of special agents to be admitted into evidence, allowing said agents to testify from memory without reference to their I-213 report, and not using said report as a cross-examination device, do not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel. Even if it is assumed that counsel should have performed all or some of the aforementioned actions, these actions (or inactions) strike us as tactical decisions, and it is not considered ineffective assistance for counsel to make tactical decisions that are not successful and operate to the client’s detriment. See Away v. Ashcroft, 328 F.3d 336, 343 (7th Cir. 2003) (hindsight applied to unwise tactical decision, to withdrawal of application for asylum, does not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel).
 
The respondent also argues that the Immigration Judge violated her due process rights by denying her “Motion for Issuance of Subpoenas for Officers’ Materials & to Recall Officers as Witnesses” and a renewed “Motion for Issuance of Subpoenas for Officers’ Materials & to Recall Officers as Witnesses” (R brief at 9-10). In particular, she argues that the Immigration Judge should not have denied these motions summarily, and that the Immigration Judge should have reviewed the officer’s materials “in camera.” We point out that the Immigration Judge did not deny the aforementioned motions summarily (Tr. at 234-39). Rather, he denied them because the respondent did not contact the special agents to see if they would attend the hearing pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1287.4(a)(2)(ii)(B) (Tr. at 235). Furthermore, the respondent’s first attorney testified that after filing a FOIA request, she had been given a compact disc from the Office of Immigration and Customs Enforcement which contained a reprint of the respondent’s file, albeit with much redaction and no notes from investigators (Tr. at 237-38). If the respondent wished to see if there was more information than was presented to her first attorney, she had a duty to try to obtain that information pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1287.4(a)(2)(ii)(B) before asking the Immigration Judge to issue a subpoena.
 
The respondent next argues that the Government did not meet its burden by clear and convincing evidence that the respondent committed marriage fraud under § 212(a)(6)(C)(I) (R at 10-13). However, we agree with the Immigration Judge that the Government did meet said burden (I.J. at 4). The Immigration Judge specifically found that the testimony of the special agents who interviewed the petitioner should be fully credited over the equivocal testimony of the petitioner and his roommate (I.J. at 10). The Immigration Judge gave reasons for his conclusion, and found that contrary to the testimony of the petitioner and his roommate, the special agents testified clearly and convincingly (I.J. at 15). These factual determinations are not “clearly erroneous.” See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (d)(3)(i)(2005) (requiring this Board to review factual determinations of an Immigration Judge for clear error); Anderson v. City of Bessemer City, North Carolina, 470 U.S. 564, 575 (1985) (emphasizing the great deference accorded to findings regarding the credibility of witnesses under the “clearly erroneous” standard of review).
 
*4 The respondent argues that the Immigration Judge should not have given more weight to the agents than to the petitioner regarding whether the petitioner signed his statement on August 6, 2004, under duress by the special agents (R brief at 10-11). We find no clear error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to accord greater weight to the special agent’s testimony than to the petitioner and his roommate (I.J. at 10, 15). There was no indication that the special agents’ testimony was in any way unreliable regarding whether the petitioner signed his statement under duress. Although the petitioner testified that the special agents told him that they would take him to jail if he didn’t talk and that he felt forced to give testimony (Tr. at 53, 73-74), one of the special agents testified that the interview was not uncomfortable, and that he wanted to impress upon the petitioner that he wasn’t being hostile (Tr. at 114-15). Furthermore, both special agents did not testify that they told the petitioner he would go to jail if he did not talk, but testified that they informed him of the consequences of marriage fraud and perjury(Tr. at 134, 164). We also agree with the Immigration Judge that the petitioner has not shown that he did not understand what he was signing in August 2004 (I.J. at 14-15). Although a physician expresses concern about the petitioner’s ability to internalize stress in a letter dated April 2006, (Exh. 3-2), the letter does not call into question the petitioner’s ability to understand what he was signing in August 2004 (I.J. at 14). The petitioner submitted an affidavit dated April 2006 (Exh. 3-1), in which he did not deny that his signature and initials appeared on the August 2004 statement, but that he was under great emotional and physical stress, and did not know what he was signing. However, we point out that one of the questions asked on the respondent’s statement (Exh. 2) was whether he understood he was giving a sworn statement and whether he swore that his statements were the truth.
 
The respondent also argues that the special agents testified strictly from memory without referring to their I-213, and that they did not enter the I-213 into evidence (R brief at 12-13). While this is true, it does not strike us as meaningful. Although the respondent argues that the government usually enters the I-213s into evidence (R brief at 12), the respondent has not provided any proof that this is true. The respondent has attempted to describe the agents’ testimony as riddled with so many inconsistencies (R brief at 11-13) that it cannot be believed regarding the crucial question as to whether her husband signed his statement under duress. However, the inconsistencies that the respondent points to (the time that the agents arrived at the apartment, the school that the petitioner’s roommate attended, and whether or not the petitioner and his roommate jointly bought the apartment that they shared together), even if true, are not material. They certainly do not render the agents’ testimony less credible regarding the petitioner’s statement (Exh. 2), and in particular, whether or not there was any duress in his signing it.
 
*5 As to the respondent’s argument that the Immigration Judge ignored as a matter of law that parties need not necessarily live together to be considered legitimately married (R brief at 13), this is not true. While the Immigration Judge pointed out that the petitioner and the respondent did not live together (I.J. at 19), this was not the main reason for the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent had engaged in marriage fraud. Furthermore, 8 C.F.R. § 1216.5(e)(2) specifically lists “documentation concerning the length of time during which the parties cohabited after the marriage and after the alien obtained permanent residence” as an element to consider when considering whether an alien entered into a qualifying marriage in good faith.
 
In conclusion, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s decision dismissing the respondent’s motion to terminate proceedings, and finding the respondent removable for procuring her admission to the United States through adjustment of status by marriage fraud under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i) of the Act.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 486949 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 486900 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED ABDUL SATER
FILE: AXX XX7 725 - DETROIT, MI
January 29, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL and MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
George P. Mann, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Rosario S. Shoudy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice; Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Asylum, withholding of removal, Convention Against Torture; reopening
 
This case is presently before us on the respondent’s timely appeal of an Immigration Judge’s November 2, 2006, order denying his motion to reopen an in absentia order of removal that was entered on August 2, 2006. Along with his appeal brief, the respondent moved to reopen this matter based upon changed country conditions and also based on a then-pending visa petition, filed by his United States citizen wife after their July 2003 marriage. The visa petition has since been approved, and a visa is immediately available to the respondent. See August 13, 2007, Supplement to Motion to Reopen.¿FN1º However, because the motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status was not filed within 90 days of the entry of the original in absentia order of removal, it is untimely and will be denied.
 
In his brief in support of his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal, the respondent argues that reopening is appropriate because ineffective assistance of prior counsel caused him to miss his scheduled hearing on August 2, 2006. Although, as the Immigration Judge indicated, the respondent was present when a hearing notice was provided to his prior counsel, he does not speak English and attests that he was never given a copy of the notice. See Immigration Judge’s November 2, 2006, Order; Respondent’s January 11, 2007, Brief at Exh. 3. Indeed, there appears to be some question about how (or if) the respondent was notified of the August 2, 2006, hearing, in that the Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) indicated below that an evidentiary hearing should be held to determine if the former attorney had, as he claimed, “called the respondent” to notify him of the August 2, 2006, hearing. See DHS’s September 18, 2006, Answer to Motion to Reopen. The respondent’s wife provided an affidavit in support of the motion to reopen below that states that the couple was unaware of the date of the August 2, 2006, hearing and that she repeatedly called the attorney’s office to find out the hearing date but was never told about it. See Respondent’s September 14, 2006, Motion to Reopen. She supports this affidavit with telephone records that purportedly indicate telephone calls to former counsel. Id. Additionally, the respondent’s wife indicated in the same affidavit that the couple did not receive notice of a prior rescheduled hearing from former counsel, and instead only learned of the new hearing date by calling the Immigration Court. Id.
 
*2 Because the respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for setting forth ineffective assistance of counsel claims, has set forth facts that, if found by the Immigration Judge, would tend to indicate ineffective assistance of counsel, and has shown prejudice from that ineffective assistance in the form of his apparent eligibility for adjustment of status, we will sustain the respondent’s appeal. Further fact- finding is necessary to determine if the respondent has set forth an actionable claim for ineffective assistance of counsel. Because we will sustain the appeal and remand this record, we need not address the respondent’s arguments regarding changed country conditions in Lebanon.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status is denied.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained and the Immigration Judge’s November 2, 2006, order denying the respondent’s motion to reopen is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded for further proceedings consistent with this order.
 
Board Member Patricia A. Cole concurs with the majority’s decision to deny respondent’s motion to reopen to pursue adjustment of status. However, the evidence of record does not support a conclusion that the respondent did not receive proper notice to appear at his removal hearing. I would not grant the respondent’s motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal and dissent from this portion of the majority’s decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD

1

2008 WL 486887 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NICOLAS HANNA DAWOUD
FILE: AXX XX9 680 - BOSTON, MA
January 28, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Saher Joseph Macarius, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s November 8, 2006, decision denying his motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The motion was denied as untimely. On appeal, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance from his former counsel, and that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
The respondent is a native and citizen of Lebanon who entered the United States on April 17, 2002, as a nonimmigrant visitor for business with authorization to remain in this country for a temporary period not to exceed June 16, 2002. He remained in the United States beyond this date without authorization, and was served with a Notice to Appear on January 9, 2003. The respondent appeared pro se at a hearing before an Immigration Judge on November 12, 2003, and explained that he married a United States citizen on June 2, 2003, and that a Petition for Alien Relative (Form I-130) was filed on his behalf. The Immigration Judge advised the respondent to submit evidence in support of his claim. The respondent subsequently retained counsel, who appeared on his behalf at a March 24, 2004, hearing. At that time, the respondent requested a continuance to await the approval of the Form I-130, and, in the alternative, sought voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge denied the request for a continuance because the respondent had failed to present evidence that a Form I-130 had been filed on his behalf, and denied voluntary departure in the exercise of discretion. The respondent filed a motion to reconsider on May 6, 2004, which was denied as untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1).
 
In an August 22, 2006, motion to reopen, the respondent argued that his removal proceedings should be reopened because his former attorney rendered ineffective assistance of counsel. In particular, he alleged that his prior counsel failed to file documents requested by the Immigration Judge, discouraged him from appealing the Immigration Judge’s decision, failed to timely file his motion to reconsider, and did not advise him of his right to file a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent’s motion to reopen was untimely. A motion to reopen before an Immigration Judge must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order of removal. See 8 C.F.R, § 1003.23(b). See section 240(c)(6) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(6); 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(1). In the instant case, the respondent’s motion would have been due on or before June 24, 2004. As noted above, the respondent did not file his motion to reopen until August 22, 2006.
 
*2 For an alien raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the untimeliness of the motion may possibly be excused under the doctrine of equitable tolling if the alien establishes that he exercised due diligence. See Cai Xing Chen v. Gonzales, 415 F.3d 151, 154 n. 3 (1st Cir. 2005) (noting the issue remains unresolved). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent has not exercised due diligence in this case. The respondent was aware of the evidence the Immigration Judge requested from him in November 2003, and that his former attorney failed to submit this evidence in support of his claim. He knew that the Immigration Judge denied his request for a continuance on March 24, 2004, and that his prior counsel did not appeal this decision. In addition, the respondent knew that the Immigration Judge denied his untimely motion to reconsider on May 6, 2004. Consequently, the conduct of his prior attorney has been known by him since mid-2004. Yet the respondent did not file the motion until nearly 2 1/2 years after the Immigration Judge’s decision. We find that these facts do not support a finding that the respondent has acted with due diligence.
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that he did not know that his former counsel was ineffective until he obtained the services of his present attorney. This argument fails. While the respondent may not have characterized his former counsel’s actions as “ineffective” as that term is used by legal practitioners in immigration proceedings, he was well aware of his counsel’s failure to submit evidence to the Immigration Court, appeal from the Immigration Judge’s March 24, 2004, decision, or file a timely motion to reconsider. Thus, he was aware of the deficiencies in his former attorney’s conduct but did not timely pursue any action regarding his conduct. Moreover, the impetus for the filing of the motion to reopen appears to be less related to any actions or conduct by the respondent’s former counsel and more related to the fact that the Form I-130 filed on his behalf was approved in September 2005. Because we find that the respondent did not act with due diligence, we agree with the Immigration Judge that his motion is not subject to equitable tolling and was properly denied as untimely. Thus, we need not address the issues of whether the respondent complied with the 1requirements for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), or whether his counsel was ineffective and he was prejudiced thereby.
 
During the pendency of the appeal, the respondent filed a motion to remand the record for consideration of his application for adjustment of status. A motion to remand filed with the Board must, among other things, state the new facts to be considered at the reopened hearing and must be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary materials demonstrating prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). In support of his motion, the respondent submitted a duplicate copy of a Notice of Approval of Relative Immigrant Visa Petition from the U.S. Citizenship and Immigration Services indicating that the Form I-130 filed on his behalf by his wife was approved on September 23, 2005. While the respondent also provided a copy of his application for adjustment of status (Form I-485), his marriage certificate, and evidence of the bona fides of his marriage, he has failed to explain why this evidence was “not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Thus, we cannot conclude that these proceedings should be remanded on the basis of “circumstances that have arisen subsequent to the bearing.” Id. Further, we note that granting the respondent’s motion would undermine the Immigration Judge’s decision denying his request for a continuance pending the adjudication of the Form I-130 filed on his behalf, and would reward him for his delay in filing evidence previously requested by the Immigration Judge. In order to avoid this unjust result, we deny the respondent’s motion in the exercise of discretion. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed and his motion is denied.
 
*3 Patricia A. Cole
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 486887 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 486872 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE SERGIO PEDRAZA-GONZALEZ
FILE: AXX XX8 139 - PHOENIX, AZ
January 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
John M. Pope, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda I. Spencer-Walters
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 In a decision dated December 13, 2006, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s removal to Mexico, should he fail to avail himself of voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge noted that any claims for relief were deemed abandoned, since no applications had been filed by the due date of August 8, 2005. The respondent has, through counsel, filed an appeal and a motion to remand. The Department of Homeland Security opposes the appeal but has not addressed the motion. Since the appeal will be sustained, the motion to remand is moot.
 
The basis for the appeal and motion, in its essence, is that the respondent was prejudiced by ineffective assistance, not of counsel, but of a paralegal employed by counsel. It is maintained that the paralegal, having been assigned to do so, failed to file the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal in a timely manner.
 
A review of the record reveals that current counsel has represented the respondent as least since May 2004. Counsel, or his partner, was present at every hearing held in the respondent’s case. At the hearing held on April 6, 2005, counsel’s partner, Mr. Stender, appeared with the respondent and was informed by the Immigration Judge (Tr. at 25) that the respondent’s application for cancellation was due no later than August 8, 2005. The respondent indicated his understanding of his duty to cooperate with counsel in the preparation of his application. The application was not timely filed.
 
In an affidavit, counsel has stated that the paralegal who was responsible for filing the application was subsequently terminated after making “many mistakes,” including failing to meet deadlines.
 
The counsel of record, not an employee with no direct representational relationship with the respondent, was ultimately responsible for the submission of any and all applications in a timely manner. Under the circumstances, the Immigration Judge’s action in this matter was not unreasonable. Rules of procedure for Immigration Courts exist, in part, to ensure the timely submission of evidence and applications in proceedings. Neither the alien nor the administrative process is well-served by the piecemeal or untimely submission of pertinent documents in a particular case, especially applications for relief.
 
Ineffective assistance cases are generally governed by the guidelines set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Azanor v. Ashcroft, 364 F.3d 1013 (9th Cir. 2004); Lata v. Immigration and Naturalization Service, 204 F.3d 1241 (9th Cir. 2000). Insufficient compliance with those guidelines has been demonstrated in this case. However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, within whose jurisdiction this case arises, has stated that the Lozada requirements “are not sacrosanct,” and that a case for ineffective assistance of counsel can be made, even without strict Lozada compliance, where the record shows a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.” See, e.g., Granados-Oseguera v. Gonzales, 464 F.3d 993 (9th Cir. 2006); Ray v. Gonzales, 439 F.3d 582 (9th Cir. 2006); Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 In our view, this case presents such a situation. The respondent was prevented a reasonable opportunity to present his case, since he was not even able to have his application for cancellation of removal considered, due to counsel’s failure to ensure that it was timely filed. Accordingly, we deem it appropriate to sustain the appeal and remand the case for the Immigration Judge to adjudicate the respondent’s cancellation application and render a decision, either granting or denying that relief.
 
In remanding this case, we intimate no opinion as to the respondent’s eligibility or worthiness for the discretionary relief he seeks.
 
ORDER: The appeal is sustained, and the Immigration Judge’s decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Court for proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion, and for the entry of a new decision.
 
Roger A. Pauley
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 486872 (BIA)

2008 WL 486870 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: XIAN SHUN SHEN
FILE: AXX XX8 045 - HARLINGEN, TX
January 25, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lauren H. Mason, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Mike Ochoa
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the February 16, 2007, decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s request for reopening of the August 30, 2006, in absentia order. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).¿FN1º
 
The respondent, a citizen and native of China, was arrested near McAllen, Texas, on June 11, 2006. Exh. 1 at 3. She admitted to being present in the United States without having been admitted or paroled after inspection. Exh. 2 at 2. The next day, she was given several documents, including a Notice to Appear at a hearing before an Immigration Judge in Harlingen, Texas, on August 30, 2006. Id.; Exh. 1 at 1. Through a Mandarin interpreter, an immigration officer also advised the respondent that she should find a good lawyer to handle her case. Exh. B at 2. The immigration officer also gave the respondent a list of free or low-cost legal service providers in the area of Harlingen-Brownsville, Texas. Id. Instead of contacting these providers, the respondent traveled to Los Angeles (where she apparently had a few friends and acquaintances). Id On or about June 23, 2006, she went to the offices of a two-attorney law firm in Los Angeles. Exh. E. While there, she spoke to an “American looking man” through a “Korean speaking lady” who served as an interpreter (the respondent’s best language is Korean). Id. The interpreter apparently first told the respondent that the documents she had indicated that she should go to court in Texas. Id. The respondent apparently asked if someone from the firm could go with her to court (although she has never indicated what the reply to this question was). Id.; Exh. B at 2. The respondent was then apparently told that she should not go to the Texas immigration court and that if she did, she would be arrested and sent back to China. Id. On appeal, the respondent contends that “¿aºlthough it is not clear that either attorney actually gave ¿meº the wrong advice, their employee did and they should be held responsible.” Respondent’s Brief at 7.
 
Where a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel is made, certain criteria must be met. Specifically, the claim must be supported by an affidavit of the respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make, counsel must be informed of the allegations against him or her and provided an opportunity to respond, and the respondent must indicate whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Mai v. Gonzales, 473 F.3d 162 (5th Cir. 2006) (citing Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497-498 (5th Cir. 2000) (upholding the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada)). It is questionable as to whether an ineffective assistance of counsel claim can be made based on representations made by nonattorney interpreters of a law firm. Regardless, the Lozada requirements have not been met in this case: the respondent has failed to identify the nonattorney employee in question, she has not shown that the “American looking man” (or any attorney affiliated with this law firm) actually agreed to represent her, and she has failed to establish that an agreement was entered into with respect to actions to be taken on her behalf.
 
*2 In short, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that the respondent’s decision to rely upon the alleged instructions to not attend the immigration hearing in Texas on August 30, 2006, is not an “exceptional circumstance” within the meaning of section 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act. I.J. at 2. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

1

The respondent’s request for a waiver of filing fees is granted. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.3(a)(1), 1003.8.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
  Case Reopened by IN RE: ANDRES JOSE CERON-ECHEVERRY, BIA, June 24, 2009
2008 WL 486850 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANDRES JOSE CERON-ECHEVERRY
FILE: AXX XX7 897 - ELOY, AZ
January 23, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on August 27, 2007, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal from an Immigration Judge’s decision that, among other things, denied his motion to terminate the removal proceedings based on his claim of derivative citizenship and ordered him removed to his native Colombia. In that decision, we concluded that the respondent failed to derive United States citizenship from his mother under former section 321(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1432, upon her naturalization on May 16, 1996, because the respondent had failed to demonstrate that his mother had actual uncontested custody of him at the time of her naturalization. On November 26, 2007, the respondent filed the instant motion to reopen his removal proceedings. The motion will be denied.
 
In his motion to reopen, the respondent initially argues that the Immigration Judge erred in denying his claim of derivative citizenship and deprived him of due process during his removal proceedings by hampering his ability to present his claim of derivative citizenship. However, in that regard, his motion is more in the nature of a motion to reconsider than a motion to reopen. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (providing that a motion to reconsider is a challenge to errors of fact or law in the Board’s decision); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 2001) (stating that motions to reopen and motions to reconsider are separate and distinct motions with different requirements). The motion is untimely because it was not filed within 30 days of our August 27, 2007, order, and the respondent has offered no explanation for his untimely filing to justify the equitable tolling of that deadline. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); cf. Ghahremani v. Gonzales, 498 F.3d 993, 999-1000 (9th Cir. 2007) (noting that an alien seeking equitable tolling of the period for filing a motion to reopen must show that he exercised due diligence in vindicating his rights). Moreover, upon review of the respondent’s arguments and all relevant portions of the record, we conclude that the respondent has failed to demonstrate exceptional circumstances that would warrant sua sponte reconsideration of our August 27, 2007, order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
The respondent also argues that reopening of his proceedings is warranted based on new evidence that was previously unavailable to him that demonstrates his eligibility for derivative citizenship. While such evidence, if present, would warrant reopening, see 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1 (c)(2), the respondent has presented no such evidence here. In that regard, most of the evidence accompanying the respondent’s motion to reopen was either already presented to the Immigration Court (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. G-K, M; see also Record of Proceedings Exh. 3, Tab D; Exh. 5, Tabs 1-7, Exh. 11, Tabs 1-7) or substantially identical in form and substance to evidence that was submitted before the Immigration Court (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. B, D; Record of Proceedings, Exh. 9). Moreover, the respondent has not shown that the “new” affidavits prepared by himself, his mother, and his godfather after our August 27, 2007, decision could not have been discovered or presented at his May 17, 2007, hearing before the Immigration Judge (Motion to Reopen, Exhs. A, C, F). See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). The respondent also has not shown that the evidence regarding his arrival and departure dates from Colombia between 1993 and 2000 could not have been discovered or presented at his May 17, 2007, removal proceedings (Motion to Reopen, Exh. E). See id.
 
*2 Finally, we reject the respondent’s contention that reopening is warranted based on ineffective assistance of counsel. As the respondent himself acknowledges, he has failed to comply with any of the procedural requirements in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (1988), for claims alleging ineffective assistance of counsel (Motion to Reopen, Affidavit of the Respondent). See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (BIA 2000) (recognizing that the Lozada requirements are generally reasonable, and that under ordinary circumstances the Board does not abuse its discretion when it denies a motion to remand or reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel where the petitioner fails to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra). Moreover, we do not find plain evidence of ineffective assistance of counsel in the record that would excuse the respondent’s noncompliance with Matter of Lozada, supra. Cf. id. at 526 (compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, not required where the record was undisputed that the alien’s lawyer failed, without any reason, to file an application for suspension of deportation in spite of having told the alien that he did file it); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir.) (compliance with Matter of Lozada, supra, not required where it was plain from the administrative record that counsel was being represented by an attorney whom she had never met and who had no understanding of her case), amended by 213 F.3d 1221 (9th Cir. 2000). An alien who raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim must demonstrate that counsel failed to perform with sufficient competence, and must establish that he was prejudiced by counsel’s performance. See Grigoryan v. Keisler, 507 F.3d 1206, 1210 (9th Cir. 2007). The respondent has failed to make that showing. Specifically, given the lack of any evidence that the respondent’s mother was awarded legal custody of him from the Colombian government following her divorce from the respondent’s father, the respondent cannot show that he sustained prejudice from his former counsel’s alleged failure to inquire whether his mother was awarded foreign legal custody of him and to advise him that a foreign court’s grant of legal custody may have changed the outcome of these proceedings. We also find no support in the record for the respondent’s general allegations that counsel failed to “keep herself abreast of relevant and significant applications and proper understanding of ¿iºmmigration ¿s ºtatutes, (Motion to Reopen at 22), that counsel “was very untruthful, unprofessional and had a serious problem with procrastinating (Motion at 23),” or that counsel hid her record of prior Florida Bar suspensions from the respondent and the Immigration Court (Motion to 23).¿FN1º
 
*3 For the foregoing reasons, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that reopening of these proceedings is warranted. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD

We note that the record shows that the respondent’s counsel did, in fact, admit to her prior suspensions at the respondent’s immigration proceedings and that the Immigration Judge rejected the Department of Homeland Security’s assertion that she had improperly failed to indicate that she was “subject” to an order of suspension by an administrative agency in her Form EOIR-28 (Notice of Entry of Appearance or Representative Before the Immigration Court) in light of those previous suspensions (Tr. at 32-34).

2008 WL 486840 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EYTAN IZYAGUYEV A.K.A. EYTAN ISIAGUEV
FILE: AXX XX7 059 - ATLANTA, GA
January 23, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
William H. Oltarsh, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decisions dated January 3, 2007, and January 9, 2007, denying the respondent’s motions to reopen. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent’s removal from the United States, following the respondent’s failure to appear for a hearing on December 6, 2006. The appeals will be dismissed.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was scheduled to appear for a hearing on December 6, 2006, in Atlanta, Georgia. On December 4, 2006, the respondent filed a motion to change venue. The respondent failed to appear for the hearing on December 6, 2006, and was ordered removed in absentia, The respondent subsequently filed a motion to reopen, in which his attorney of record, William H. Oltarsh, Esquire, stated that his own spouse was suffering from terminal cancer, and that he failed to advise the respondent that he had to appear for his hearing on December 6, 2006. With the motion to reopen, the respondent submitted a sworn statement requesting that venue be changed to New York, New York. However, the respondent failed to provide any explanation for his failure to appear at the hearing on December 6, 2006. The Immigration Judge found that the attorney’s filings admit actual notice of the hearing, and failure to notify the respondent is not a basis for reopening absent a Lozada affidavit. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). On appeal, Mr. Oltarsh has submitted a sworn statement in which he indicates that he believes he informed the respondent that he was required to appear for a master calendar hearing in Atlanta, Georgia, but Mr. Oltarsh cannot remember the exact details due to his wife’s terminal illness.
 
The Board has held that the mere filing of a motion for a change of venue does not relieve the respondent of his responsibility to appear, and unless the Immigration Judge grants the motion, the respondent remains obligated to appear at the appointed date and time. Matter of Rivera, 19 I&N Dec. 688 (BIA 1988); Matter of Patel, 19 I&N Dec. 260 (BIA 1985), aff’d, Patel v. INS, 803 F.2d 804 (5th Cir. 1986). Furthermore, we have held that ineffective assistance of counsel can constitute an “exceptional circumstance” for purposes of rescission of an in absentia order where the alien establishes that the failure to appear was the result of ineffective assistance of counsel and where the criteria set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, are satisfied. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec.472 (BIA 1996); see also Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996). The respondent has not asserted that he failed to appear due to ineffective assistance of counsel, nor has he submitted an affidavit in support of such a claim. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent had proper notice of his hearing, and an attorney’s failure to notify a client is not a basis for reopening absent compliance with Lozada. As the respondent failed to submit an affidavit in support of his motion to reopen pursuant to Lozada, the Immigration Judge correctly determined that reopening is not warranted in this matter.
 
*2 The respondent also appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated January 9, 2007, denying the respondent’s second motion to reopen filed on January 4, 2007. The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the exceptional circumstances must relate to the respondent, such that the illness of counsel’s spouse does not constitute an exceptional circumstance. Furthermore, the respondent’s second motion to reopen is number-barred pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Accordingly, the respondent’s appeals are dismissed.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 486840 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 339634 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTURO TIMOTEO REYES-REYES A.K.A. ARTURO T. REYES A.K.A. ARTURO TIMOTEO A.K.A. AUTHORO R. REYES A.K.A. ARTURO LINARES A.K.A. RUBEN MONTICELLO
FILE: AXX XX6 020 - CHICAGO, IL
January 16, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Isuf Kola, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brendan Curran
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent, a native and citizen of Mexico, has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated September 10, 2007. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable and found him ineligible for relief from removal based on his criminal conviction.
 
This Board reviews an Immigration Judge’s findings of fact, including findings as to the credibility of testimony, under the “clearly erroneous” standard. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(i) (2007). This Board reviews questions of law, discretion, and judgment in an Immigration Judge’s decision de novo. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3)(ii) (2007).
 
The respondent was convicted of Retail Theft on July 24, 2006. He was sentenced to probation. Because he violated probation, he was resentenced to two years in jail. The Immigration Judge found the respondent removable for having an aggravated felony relating to theft.
 
On appeal, the respondent alleges that removability was not proved. The Immigration and Nationality Act provides that “Any alien … in and admitted to the United States shall, upon the order of the Attorney General, be removed if the alien is within one or more of the following classes of deportable aliens …. (iii) Aggravated Felony.--Any alien who is convicted of an aggravated felony at any time after admission is deportable.” See 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii) (1996). The term “aggravated felony” includes a theft offense (including receipt of stolen property) or burglary offense for which the term of imprisonment ¿isº at least one year.” See 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(G) (1997).
 
Within the record is an information charging the respondent with Retail Theft and a certified statement of conviction and disposition. That document indicates that the respondent pled guilty to the charge and was sentenced to probation on July 24, 2006. It also indicates that the respondent was found guilty of violating probation and sentenced to two years on April 24, 2007. We find clear and convincing evidence of removability. See Woodby v. INS, 385 U.S. 276, 286 (1966); 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c)(3) (1997); 8 C.F.R. § 1240.8(a) (2007). Cf. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.15, 1240.10 (2007).
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent also disputes that he received a two-year jail sentence for the theft offense. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the imposition of a sentence of imprisonment following revocation of probation is a modification of the original sentence. See U.S. v. Hidalgo-Marcias, 300 F.3d 281, 284-85 (2nd Cir. 2002) (conviction for attempted burglary became an aggravated felony after probation was revoked and sentenced to an additional one-year sentence); U.S. v. Jimenez, 258 F.3d 1120, 1125-26 (9th Cir. 2001) (conviction for infliction of corporal injury on a spouse became an aggravated felony after probation was revoked and sentenced to two years).
 
Also on appeal, the respondent claims he has received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent has failed to fulfil the prerequisites for allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). See also Stroe v. INS, 256 F.3d 498, 501 (7th Cir. 2001).
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
Daniel Lippman
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 339634 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 339641 (BIA)

Board Of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: WILLIAM INNOCENTE
FILE: AXX XX3 884 - OAKDALE, LA
January 16, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Mario DeMarco, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Craig A. Harlow
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
Sec. 237(a)(2)(B)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(B)(i)º - Convicted of controlled substance violation
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We affirm the Immigration Judge’s July 26, 2007, decision concluding that the respondent, a native and citizen of Italy, is removable and ineligible for cancellation of removal as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony. Sections 237(a)(2)(A)(iii) and 240A(a)(3) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1227(a)(2)(A)(iii), 1229b(a)(3)(2000).
 
During his removal hearing, the respondent admitted through counsel that he was removable as charged. (Tr. 2). Having conceded that he is removable as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony, it necessarily follows that he cannot demonstrate the absence of an aggravated felony conviction in connection with his cancellation of removal application. Applicable regulations, which have the force and effect of law as to the Immigration Judges and this Board, provide in pertinent part that “the immigration judge may determine that removability as charged has been established by the admissions of the respondent.” 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c) (2007). Accordingly, the distinct and formal concession of removability, made by the respondent’s attorney acting in his professional capacity and in the respondent’s presence during the removal hearing, is binding on the respondent as a judicial admission, and he will not now be heard to challenge the factual findings or legal conclusions of the Immigration Judge that arose from that concession. Matter of Velasquez, 19 I&N Dec. 377, 382 (BIA 1986); cf. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(B) (authorizing summary dismissal of appeals when “¿tºhe only reason for the appeal specified by the party concerned involves a finding of fact or a conclusion of law that was conceded by that party at a prior proceeding”). Furthermore, the binding effect of an attorney’s concession of removability applies with equal force in a proceeding predicated on criminal convictions. Matter of Pichardo, 21 I&N Dec. 330, 333 n.2 (BIA 1996). Finally, the respondent has not argued or proven that his prior attorney’s concession of removability constituted ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N.Dec. 637 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 In conclusion, we find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision ordering the respondent removed from the United States on the basis of his attorney’s concession of removability. Because the respondent is removable and ineligible for cancellation of removal on the basis of his concession, we do not address his appellate arguments regarding the validity of the aggravated felony charge or the Immigration Judge decision pretermitting his cancellation of removal application.
 
The appeal is dismissed.
 
Lauri S. Filppu
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 339641 (BIA)
End of Document

2008 WL 243746 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARGARITA ARCEGA
FILE: AXX XX5 809 - BALTIMORE, MD
January 11, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rachel S. Ullman, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dimple R. Shah
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On May 21, 2004, the Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under sections 240A(b)(l)(B) and (2)(A)(iii) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C.§§ 1229b(b)(l)(B) and (2)(A)(iii), based on a determination that the respondent was not a person of good moral character during the requisite periods. On July 20, 2005, the Board summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal for failure to file a brief.¿FN1º
 
The respondent did not comply with the final order of removal. Rather, she continued to work without authorization as a nanny. The respondent filed the instant motion to reopen on July 5, 2007, after the Department of Homeland Security sought to enforce the order of removal. The motion is untimely. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent contends that the time limit for filing her motion should be equitably tolled because she received ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also contends that the Board should reopen proceedings sua sponte, or under section 240 (c)(7)(C)(iv) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229 (c)(7)(C)(iv). In support of her motion, the respondent has submitted a large number of documents, as well as two supplemental motions with evidence. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS”) opposes reopening.
 
The respondent has substantially complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (lst Cir. 1988). See Barry v. Gonzales, 445 F.3d 741, 745-46 (4th Cir. 2006). The respondent contends that former counsel provided ineffective assistance at the hearing, on appeal, and failed to provide her notice of the Board’s decision. In support of her claims, the respondent’s current employer asserts that the respondent’s file did not include letters from former counsel regarding the pending appeal or the Board’s decision. The respondent also contends that she made payments after the Board dismissed the appeal, which supports her assertion that she did not have notice of the Board’s decision.
 
a review of the record does not indicate that the respondent’s former counsel provided ineffective assistance at either the hearing or on appeal. The fact that the respondent did not obtain relief, the denial of which was based on her own misrepresentations, does not mean that her former counsel was ineffective. Further, we agree with the Disciplinary Board of the Supreme Court of Pennsylvania that the respondent’s former counsel did not provide ineffective assistance during her appeal because her former counsel contacted her during the pendency of her appeal, but she did not respond or pay her fees in accordance with his letter dated April 6, 2005. Moreover, after the Board issued its decision, the respondent’s former counsel mailed a copy of the decision to the respondent at her last known address. See Motion to Reopen, Exh. C, Letter dated July 24, 2005. The respondent’s assertions regarding lack of notice are given minimal weight, given that the Immigration Judge previously found that she misrepresented facts to obtain an immigration benefit, that she concealed information, and was not forthcoming (I.J. Dec. dated 5/21/04 at 3-4). The fact that the respondent did not provide copies of her former attorney’s letters to her current employer does not establish lack of notice. Similarly, making several payments of $100 to former counsel after the Board’s decision was issued does not necessarily establish that the respondent did not have notice of our decision. Further, the respondent failed to demonstrate that she exercised due diligence in seeking reopening. See Chao v. Virginia Department of Transportation, 291 F.3d 276, 283 (4th Cir. 2002)(equitable tolling is not available when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf). The Immigration Judge issued his decision in 2004, we summarily dismissed the appeal in 2005, and the respondent was provided with notice of our decision soon thereafter, but did not hire new counsel until after the DHS contacted her in 2007. Even if the respondent did not have notice of our decision, her assertions of due diligence are not plausible given the passage of time from the Immigration Judge’s decision to the filing of the instant motion, the Immigration Judge’s prior findings of misrepresentation, former counsel’s assertions that he attempted to contact the respondent during the pendency of the appeal, but the respondent did not respond to his telephone calls, and the follow-up letter from the respondent’s former counsel mailed during the pendency of the appeal. The respondent’s motion to reopen under section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) is also denied because it is based on the same allegations and facts previously presented to, and rejected by, the Immigration Judge. We do not view section 240(c)(7)(C)(iv) as an opportunity for an illegal alien to relitigate the same issues previously presented and rejected. Further, the respondent’s current motion under section 240(C)(7)(C)(iv) is untimely, and a review of the record, including the motion to reopen, and supplements, do not provide sufficient evidence of extraordinary circumstances or extreme hardship to her children so as to justify an exception to the time-bar. We decline, moreover, to reopen sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999) (fundamental change in law may warrant sua sponte reopening notwithstanding otherwise applicable time and number limitations on motions); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)(Board retains limited discretionary power under the regulations to reopen or reconsider only in exceptional circumstances).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD

1

Summary dismissal was appropriate because the Notice of Appeal did not adequately notify the Board of the factual and legal reasons for the appeal as required by 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(b).

2008 WL 243758 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JIAN GUO CHEN
FILE: AXX XX6 741 - NEW YORK, NY
January 10, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
June Zhou, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James D. Paoli
Senior Attorney
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 This case was last before us on December 29, 2005, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s March 30, 2005, denial of a motion to reopen and rescind an in absentia removal order issued on February 25, 2005.
 
On November 30, 2007, the respondent, a native and citizen of China, filed a motion to reopen and rescind the in absentia removal order. Under section 240(b)(5)(C)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C)(i), an in absentia removal order may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of exceptional circumstances. The respondent acknowledges that his motion is untimely, but seeks equitable tolling of the 180-day deadline because his motion is premised on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel against his former attorney, Mr. Xu.
 
The respondent’s motion will be denied for two reasons. First, it is untimely filed. The respondent seeks equitable tolling of the 180-day motions deadline based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, has held that the deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iavorski v. United States INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2nd Cir. 2000). Aliens relying on equitable tolling must establish that (1) their constitutional right to due process was violated by counsel’s conduct; and (2) they exercised due diligence during the period sought to be tolled. Id.
 
We find that equitable tolling is not warranted in this case where the respondent has failed to exercise due diligence in pursing his case. The respondent’s prior motion to reopen, which was also premised on an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, was denied two years ago because the respondent failed to comply with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Nor is this motion supported by evidence that he has complied with Lozada. He has simply failed to show that he exercised due diligence during the period sought to be tolled. See Cekic v. INS, 435 F.3d 167 (2nd Cir. 2006) (finding that aliens had demonstrated that their counsel was ineffective in informing them that they did not need to appear at a hearing, but they had not met their burden of showing why they had not been diligent over a two-year period).
 
*2 The respondent’s motion to reopen these removal proceedings will also be denied as number barred. Federal regulations at 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(iii)(D) provide an exception to the one-motion rule when a motion seeking to rescind an in absentia order is filed. However, that exception is limited to motions that are filed in deportation and exclusion proceedings. There is no equivalent exception in removal proceedings. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c)(3) (providing an exception only to the time, but not to the numerical, restrictions on motions in removal proceedings); 1003.23(b)(4)(h), (iii)(D). Thus, unless another exception is shown to apply, the respondent’s second motion is number barred.
 
Based on the foregoing, the respondent’s motion is denied as time and number barred.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
 
Frederick D. Hess
FOR THE BOARD
2008 WL 243758 (BIA)
End of Document

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board Of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RICARDO DE PARAMO
FILE: AXX XX0 262 - MIAMI, FL
January 3, 2008
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
  
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linda Osberg-Braun, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. By decision mailed August 7, 2007, the Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia, and thereafter on October 1, 2007, denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent has filed a timely Notice of Appeal from the latter decision. The matter will be remanded.
 
The respondent contends that he filed a change of address with the Department of Homeland Security prior to the initiation of these proceedings, and that the DHS thus erred in not sending the Notice to Appear to his new, correct address, an error repeated by the Immigration Court when it sent the notice for the August 7, 2007, hearing date. Alternatively, the respondent contends that his order of removal in absentia should be rescinded because his former counsel provided ineffective assistance of counsel in failing to correct the address error, and in failing to enter an appearance with the Immigration Court, which would have resulted in counsel receiving the hearing notice.
 
We find the respondent’s first argument unavailing. He admits to having received the Notice to Appear (NTA), which was sent to his former address and forwarded to him by the Postal Service. At that point, he was on notice that the address on the NTA was defective, and he was under obligation, as clearly stated in the NTA, to notify the Immigration Court of his current address. His failure to do so, which resulted in the sending of the Notice of Hearing for August 7, 2007, cannot be excused under the law of the applicable Circuit nor of this Board. Dominguez v. United States Att’y Gen’l, 284 F.3d 1258 (11th Cir. 2002); Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181 (BIA 2001). The respondent’s allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are first presented on appeal, and their resolution will require findings of fact that are best resolved in the first instance by the Immigration Judge. The respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), with respect to Mr. Soto, his former counsel. While there is evidence that the respondent hired Mr. Soto to represent him in “deportation” proceedings, and while the respondent contends on appeal that he informed Mr. Soto of the initiation of these removal proceedings, there is no response as yet from Mr. Soto. The Immigration Judge on remand shall re-consider the respondent’s motion to reopen the in absentia order of removal in light of the new claims presented regarding alleged ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Accordingly, the Immigration Judge’s decision is affirmed with respect to the issues presented in the respondent’s initial motion to reopen, and the proceedings are remanded for further consideration of that motion in light of the new allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
Edward R. Grant

2007 WL 4818611 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MUHAMMAD SARFRAZ KHAN
FILE: AXX XX8 104 - HOUSTON, TX
December 31, 2007
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Amen Omo Obas, Esquire¿FN1º
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Gerrie Zhang
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated August 17, 2005. On August 17, 2005, the Board affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s request for a continuance based on a pending application for labor certification, denied a motion to remand for adjustment of status based on the approval of the labor certification of the respondent’s wife on April 11, 2005, and reinstated a grant of voluntary departure. On October 28, 2005, we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our decision on the merits. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. The respondent’s motion to reopen, filed on August 2, 2007, seeks reopening for consideration of adjustment of status and/or cancellation of removal and is opposed by the Department of Homeland Security (DHS). The motion is denied as untimely filed.
 
The respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). We find that under the facts alleged ineffective assistance of counsel has not been shown. In the complaint filed with the Texas Bar, the respondent asserts, inter alia, that his former attorney did not timely file an appeal of his case with this Board. However, the record reveals that the appeal was, in fact, timely filed. In the motion to reopen, the respondent contends that his former attorney should have filed a timely motion to reopen for consideration of cancellation of removal and/or adjustment of status. The only potential qualifying relative for cancellation of removal mentioned in the current motion is the respondent’s wife, who became a lawful permanent resident on March 9, 2006, more than 90 days after our August 17, 2005, decision. Consequently, the respondent could not have shown that he had prima facie eligibility for cancellation of removal or adjustment of status so as to timely file a motion to reopen. Contrary to the respondent’s contentions, the Board itself and Immigration Judges are without authority to apply the doctrine of equitable estoppel against the DHS so as to preclude it from undertaking a lawful course of action that it is empowered to pursue by statute and regulation. See Matter of Hernandez-Puente, 20 I&N Dec. 335, 338 (BIA 1991). Moreover, we also decline to reopen sua sponte. Simply acquiring additional equities after a final order of removal does not establish an exceptional situation that would support circumventing the regulations where enforcing them might result in hardship. The f£.ct that the respondent’s wife is now a lawful permanent resident and he is the beneficiary of a pending visa petition for derivative adjustment does not provide a basis for sua sponte reopening.See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied as untimely, and the request for a stay of removal is denied.
 
David B. Holmes
FOR THE BOARD

Amen Omo Obas, Esquire, has not submitted a motion to withdraw as counsel. Consequently, we consider Amen Omo Obas as the attorney of record.
